RATING: 8
Lots of freedom and very little downtime. This game has a good tempo to it. There's two reasons it's not a 9. Firstly it's a little bit too open/sandboxy to fully satisfy my taste. Secondly it's so non-confrontational that it almost feels like playing solitaire (it makes games like Suburbia and Race for the Galaxy, both of which I love, seem like all out war).

I have to point out that I strongly disagree with the people who talk of this game as having a significant luck factor. It might seem so after one play or two, but it's really not that hard to stay in control. You can let pieces stay on the board instead of forcing it to play as many actions as possible each turn. That way you you can plan ahead and keep the randomness slightly lower. You can also get rid of discs by doing good deeds.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Very thinly themed game occurring in a time period when the French were fighting the 100 year war.  What makes this game work is the variability, the unique "bag building" mechanic, and the ease of play.

The game occurs over 18 rounds, which sounds like a lot but actually plays very quickly.  Each round, the following happens: 1) flip a random event (very similar to Pillars of the Earth where some are good and more are bad), this event will occur later in the round so you have some time to plan for it, 2) reward and penalize the most and least industrious farmers (gave a gold or lose a gold), 3) draw and assign your workers to your own private action board.  This is the meat of the game, you have a bag that you can potentially fill (or even thin) with workers of varying types and these all have specific powers/rewards whenever you gain them and different action spaces require certain workers (colour of worker, red, white, brown, etc.).  The actions are typically get more workers of a type and gain the corresponding bonus (and you want more of EVERYTHING because it's all good!), travel on the map in order to gain valuable goods, or build trading posts on the map which are worth end game points and for which space is limited and first come first serve.  You can also assign workers to go away and perform feats/jobs for which they receive a small reward but you never get them back (you want to do this to a) thin your bag, b) gain the small bonuses, c) potentially snipe the larger citizen bonus). 4) after workers are placed, they are assigned, which essentially means you fill up your board as best you can and when it's your turn choose to activate an action (or not).  5) finally resolve the event, then pass the start player, rinse and repeat.

Points are scored for: a) money (and no this is not pittance points, it can be VERY significant), b) goods (they range from 1-5 points each), c) the sum of your built trading posts and citizens (not workers) times your multiplier on the prestige track (this is awarded primarily through acquiring and activating the gray worker).  There are also a large selection of unique special buildings, ala Caverna, that can be acquired through the black worker, and several of these can be used to score you points (they're essentially private action spaces).

The game plays quickly and smoothly as long as everyone assigns workers simultaneously (being able to adjust last minute if they're later in turn order) and once familiarity with the special buildings is attained.  It seems to play very well, albeit a bit differently from 2-4 players, and there is an option to play 5 (not sure I would want to due to downtime).  Seems to be several ways to play it/specialize and lots of potential for expansions (in the way of special buildings).  Very nice components provided you have upgraded wooden pieces.

Own the [boardgame=183682]Invasion[/boardgame], and the [boardgame=204814]Trade & Intrigue[/boardgame] expansions and the [boardgame=179417]Neue Ortskarten #1[/boardgame], [boardgame=184018]Neue Ortskarten #2[/boardgame], [boardgame=185922]Neue Ortskarten #3[/boardgame], [boardgame=211082]Neue Ortskarten #4[/boardgame], [boardgame=237163]Neue Ortskarten #5[/boardgame] and the [boardgame=189623]Orleans: Christmas Market Advent Calendar[/boardgame] and [boardgame=210145]Prairie[/boardgame] promo tiles.

RATING: 8.5
First play rating, so may change over time. Excellent bag-building game with elegant rules and nice depth of decision-making. The game is easy to teach and interesting throughout. Our game came down to a one point difference. The artwork is a little on the dull side and components aren't anything to write home about, but there's plenty of 3rd party upgrades. The game itself is rock solid and no one path immediately seems more effective than another. Turns are fast and the game plays very smoothly. Overall, lovely.

RATING: 8.5
Great bag buidling system.

8.2 Points only with 

1) the official correction for the "Badehaus" 

2) Harvest house rule, which creates some extra tension and makes weak bzuildings stronger:
You have to pay one food the first time (normal rule), but 2 the 2nd time and 3 the third time.
No harvest event can follow directly another harvest event (in case, reshuffle and draw again)

3) in case of not enough coins left to pay out an event, don't pay out to anyone (instead start player first).
Alternatively play with unlimited coin supply

4) free promo Tiles "Tavern" and "Depot" included in the game

Still rooom for improvent in several detals and the buildings (cheesery and hayrick much too weak e.g.)

Could rise even to a 9 with expansion, if some details like the above are fixed and potential in buildings and events is better used.

Update: Get the Expansion "Trade & Intrigue", which fixes most of the weaknesses the base game had, then you can forget what I wrote above. So it's a 9 point game with that expansion (Event module and extra locations, maybe contract module, too, new deeds board optional (but not the intrigue one, which is crazy). If you use new deeds board, Game should be shortened 1-2 rounds.

RATING: 7
6 plays / 4,5 players

LIKE: Even though it's just another efficiency race game, there is lots to explore. The multitude of strategies is very fun and the main point of the game. Gameplay was smooth. Nice thick tokens (TMG version). Good 5 player game (I have additional player pieces).

DISLIKE: Money wasn't tight, so the events seemed pointless. Goods gained weren't used for anything but points, so that felt bland. Lacks player interaction at times. Rules weren't complete, felt they left some questions unanswered.

OVERALL: Good game, but I am surprised this is rated as high as it is. I do like it because of the sheer amount of strategies to pursue, but the lack of tension in the game gave it a somewhat mild aftertaste. I am eager to expansions to make the game a little edgier. (Upgraded rating after a few plays.)

RATING: 6
Orléans is a quite nice engine building game. You start with limited possibilities but continuously expand your horizons. I especially enjoyed that the game rewards long term strategies, i.e., at the start you choose the way you want to go and push it all the way through. This is really nice since you can make a great master plan, and then just try to execute it.

It seems that most strategies are more or less equally competitive meaning that the outcome will be decided on the tactical level. You need to phase your actions so that you always get what you need at the right time. Some strategies involve more direct competition with the opponents while others allow for you to fondle your own stuff mostly. This might be an issue especially with four players if three decide to race against each other while the fourth player can proceed at a pace of his own.

At first, you might think that the game would be awfully long with 18 full rounds. However, the 90 minutes playing time is actually quite true provided that everyone allocates their workers on action spaces simultaneously. I really think this is the only valid way to play, since 18 rounds would just be too long if everyone pondered every single move for a few minutes. The setup is a bit long, though.

One decision I did not fancy, though, was that all extra buildings are available right from the start. This creates an unnecessary vast decision tree and leaves novices and beginner level players flabbergasted. Moreover, it is easy to think of a tight game with experts who want to analyze everything very carefully and thus freezing the game due to too many options available.

In a nutshell, I enjoyed Orléans but there is nothing exceptional to it. There are some (minor) issues with the vastness of the decision tree but you can adopt a strategy which allows you to take a more straightforward approach and ignore the tree completely.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
The term "bag builder" (as opposed to a "deck builder") is often used to describe this game, and the term is apt.  Many of the same thought processes go into playing this game that go into deck building games like Dominion.  As in Dominion, putting together a particularly effective combination of workers in your bag can lead to a strong engine that can crush the opposition, but can also lead to repetitive play that doesn't require much thinking once the engine is humming.  Unlike base-game Dominion, there are things the opponents can do to slow you down and try to indirectly disrupt your engine.

The game is interesting and has a feeling of progression as you put together an engine, but if you are able to get things running too smoothly, you can spend much of the game doing the same thing over and over again, which is tedious.  This isn't always the case, however, and there are many paths to take toward victory in the game.

All in all, Orleans is a solid, mid-weight Euro with an interesting blend of mechanisms.

RATING: 9.1
I think this is my favorite, "Gateway to non-gateway games game".  It takes a solid 20 minutes to explain the game to someone, as long as they know something about games, but the game play is actually pretty simple.  The funny thing is that a new player will not know how terrible they are at it until the end, when the scores are all added up.  Every time I play, I figure out a little more about how to build points, which revolves around mitigating the randomness of drawing tiles from your bag every round.  

My only complaint is the same complaint I have with games like Puerto Rico.  It's a little too similar to Chess in the way that, a new player has almost no chance at doing well the first few plays.  And the really experienced players can get WAY too caught up in trying to have perfect strategy.  The only solution is to just have fun with it and not pay attention to those who say you're doing it wrong.  You're probably doing it wrong, but who cares, have fun)

I know that this competitiveness is what causes a lot of people to say they don't like teaching the game to new players.  But I would much rather teach it to someone new than play against an Orleans grand-master who has spent years developing the perfect path to the most points.

RATING: 7
Πολύ ευχάριστο eurogame που συνδυάζει γνωστούς και δοκιμασμένους μηχανισμούς όπως το deck building με το worker placement. Ουσιαστικά στο παιχνίδι όλοι ξεκινούν με 4 εργάτες τους οποίους τους τοποθετείς στο προσωπικό σου ταμπλό για να ενεργοποιήσεις κάποια actions. Σταδιακά αποκτάς και άλλους εργάτες, χτίζεις κτήρια, ταξιδεύεις στο χάρτη, μαζεύεις προϊόντα και άλλα κλασσικά euro πράγματα. 

Οι εργάτες είναι σε διάφορα χρώματα και για κάθε action απαιτείτε συγκεκριμένος συνδυασμός χρωμάτων. Κάθε γύρα τραβάς από το σακούλι σου ορισμένους εργάτες και τους τοποθετείς όπου κρίνεις εσύ χρήσιμο. Αν δεν ολοκληρώσεις το συνδυασμό των χρωμάτων μπορείς να αφήσεις τους εργάτες εκεί, με την ελπίδα ότι την επόμενη γύρα θα τραβήξεις το χρώμα που σου λείπει.

Από την στιγμή που ο καθένας φτιάχνει την μηχανή του και παίζει στο προσωπικό του ταμπλό το interaction είναι αρκετά περιορισμένο. Υπάρχει όμως ο χάρτης της Γαλλίας στον οποίο οι παίκτες μετακινούνται και ανοίγουν καταστήματα (χωράει ένα σε κάθε πόλη οπότε όποιος προλάβει χτίζει) ενώ και ο αριθμός των εργατών είναι μικρός για κάθε χρώμα.

Σε κάθε γύρο συμβαίνει και ένα random event, το οποίο γίνεται γνωστό από την αρχή του γύρου και έτσι προλαβαίνεις να προετοιμαστείς. Υπάρχει και μια δόση τύχης στο παιχνίδι αφού κάθε γύρα τραβάς τυχαία τους εργάτες σου.

Στα θετικά να βάλω πως υπάρχουν διάφοροι τομείς για να ασχοληθεί κάποιος, οπότε το παιχνίδι έχει ψωμί αλλά από την άλλη δεν είμαι σίγουρος για το replayability καθώς κάθε φορά όλα τα κτήρια είναι διαθέσιμα. Επίσης δεν με ικανοποίησε η ποιότητα παραγωγής. Τόσο δύσκολο θα ήταν οι εργάτες να ήταν ξύλινα δισκάκια αντί για tokens??

Μας πήρε 2 και κάτι ώρες για μια παρτίδα τεσσάρων παικτών.

Γενικά το παιχνίδι μου άφησε μια ευχάριστη αίσθηση και δεν θα έλεγα όχι σε μια παρτίδα. Απλά κάποιες λεπτομέρειες δεν το αφήνουν να ξεχωρίσει. Για κάποιο λόγο πιστεύω πως θα μπορούσε να είναι πολύ καλύτερο από ότι είναι. Ίσως φταίει που δεν είναι αρκετά σφιχτό και πως τα events είναι πολύ χλιαρά.

RATING: 8
A tile drafting game that feels fresh, original and amusing to play.  I had a lot of fun playing it for the first time.

Orleans feels like a mellow combination of Dominion and Castles of Burgundy with additional "special buildings," "traveling on a map and building houses," and climbing on the various bonus tracks to get benefits.

Bottom line:  I think Orleans is a fun, worthy addition to the euro game library.  Should be in the top 200 at least.

RATING: 8
Orleans is a worker drafting and placement game with various ways to collect victory points. Use different combinations of workers to obtain additional more valuable workers, who in turn help up upgrade you farming, progress, civil, maritime, castle and university skills. Each of these skills provides different ways to obtain victory points. All of your goods, money and properties earn victory points at the end...most VP wins!

RATING: 8.8
The bag building in this game adds a layer of fun and chance that you don't often experience in comparable Euro games. The actions are all smooth and intuitive, the only learning curve is just remembering what they each do, learning the tiles and how you rank up along the various tracks when claiming the different worker tokens. Cute art, and this is ultimately a good medium weight game for those who have 'graduated' from gateway worker placement, say...Stone Age, with the bag mechanic here replacing that minor element of randomness from the die rolling. Believe the hype, this is worth your while.

RATING: 8
3/21/16: This game is a lot of fun, and plays very smoothly.  Need to get the expansions from DLP.  With upgraded DLP components this is beautiful. 

A deck builder without cards.  You throw your acquired meeples into a draw bag.  There is a separate board where you can permanently place your less valuable meeples to "thin your deck."  The variety in the game comes from the different buildings that are used each time.

Certainly not a heavy game, but lots of fun.  I think that this was one of my favorite games at Gamestorm, and playing it there convinced me to pick it up.

Want to order the following from DLP: a) Fan Kit, b) Ostkarten #2, c) Ostkarten #3 4) Scorepad

Plays well with 4 and 5, but 5 makes the board crowded.

RATING: 7
This is the most impressive game I played at EuroQuest 2014. It essentially is a medium-weight point-salad that has a healthy amount of tension as players race to certain VP-scoring opportunities. But it has a "deck-building" element where players draw action tokens out of a bag, which were added in previous turns; thus, the random element is strongly influenced by the decisions you made earlier (Action tokens you purchased or kept on the board). There is also an event deck but those cards tend to affect players at the end of the round. So the end result is a game that provides meaningful decisions and handles/controls randomness very well. 

Component wise... not the best. The browns/blacks are too close together, the action tokens are obnoxiously thin to pick up, and the game did seem to age worst than other games (aka... ink coming off, seams on player board showing, etc). Is it bad... no. Could it be better... yes. I'd say gamers would be very please with this 5-10 years ago but with publishers pushing industry standards... it ends up looking average.

RATING: 6
I liked the idea of the bag building mechanism, and even enjoyed the game for some time. However I do have a couple of issues with the game. First of all it overstayed its welcome. Everybody said it played a bit too long (2h with 3 players). 18 rounds turned out to be too long. At the beginning of the game you have many options, but can only do 1-2 actions a rounds. Later in the game you have a lot of capabilities, but a number of actions diminishes, which is irritating. On the other hand if it weren't for this aspect interaction would be even scarcer. And this smoothly leads us to the second problem. This is mainly a multiplayer solitaire. Hardly ever do you interact with other players. The map where you wander with your merchant is so big that all 3 players easily maneuvered over there. Only by the end of the game did we look at the Town's Hall to see whether somebody is about to snatch the citizen of the auxiliary board. Overall, I like euro games and indirect interaction, but this one lacked interaction even for my taste.

Overall, it was an ok game, but there are small chances it will hit the table again, there are simply better picks for this time span. I have just put it on our local Polish MathTrade.

RATING: 7.5
"Bag building" seem the theme of 2014. And Orleans is probably the game that best implements the mechanic.

Every turn players draw a certain number of followers from their bag, and use them to "activate" various different locations, giving them more followers or moving a merchant on a map, or gaining various kinds of goods or money.

There are twenty buildings with various special powers: when players acquire a building they have a unique access to it, and to a more powerful version of one of the basic actions, or to a different unique power.

The game is charming and engaging, and the only critique I can move is the lacking of a true engine building: most activities let players earn goods or money, and these are basically just different ways of define victory points.

A very good game: initial tentative rating is 7.5.

RATING: 8
Initial Impression: So far I've only played this as a cooperative game but my first impression is very favorable. I'm a sucker for a well implemented deckbuilding mechanism and this game has certainly hit the target. There's a feeling of multiple options for every chit pulled from the bag, and you never feel as if your pull was useless. Thematic mechanisms are also in place to "thin your deck" when needed. Although the cooperative game has no "AI", the targets needded to win are challenging and require coordination from every player in order to succeed. This game is slick, flows quickly, and is easy to teach.

RATING: 6
Orleans is a game that, to win, you must implement an effective strategy. However, the game still gives you, from round to round, a sense of accomplishment and progression if you happen to play badly. Combine the sense of progression with its ease of teaching and you have an accessible strategy game. Additionally, through the bag pulls, each round provides some tactical-level decision making. Interaction with other players is largely subtle and turn-order based. For example, you race to complete areas on the town hall board before other players or race nearby players to build trading depots. 

The game offers you a number of strategies to pursue, but places all the burden on the players to make Orleans feel replayable and different from play to play. The game play of Orleans does not force players to try those different strategies. You will also be funneled towards diversifying in at least some ways, as it does not seem viable to employ a strategy that completely ignores certain point-scoring aspects of the game. 

Every non-worker item you collect in the base game is, basically, a representation of a certain number of victory points. Due to this, Orleans feels hollow -- you go through the game's motions simply because the game wants you to and not because you want to achieve something aside from victory points. As mapped to the game's mechanisms, you move forward round to round collecting workers, collecting resources, building trading depots, and the like, but you only do so because that is the way you collect victory points. 

Interestingly, the Invasion expansion and the upcoming Trade & Intrigue expansion appear to make attempts at addressing the preceding paragraph's criticism. The core mechanisms of Orleans are interesting enough that I look forward to trying out Trade & Intrigue when the TMG version is released, and I will be attempting some Invasion content very soon. However, I expected more out of the base game.

Edit, Sept 2017: Invasion bumps this rating up a bit, but it changes the overall feel of Orleans enough that I rated Invasion on its own. I rate Invasion a strong 7, but base Orleans a 6.

RATING: 6
I had high hopes for this one, but after a few plays it dawned on me that it won't stay long.

The bag-building is interesting and could surely be built upon. However, for my taste Orleans [b]really[/b] lacks long-term replay-value.
Only the map part of the board has some randomness when setting up the game. Unfortunately the travelling part of the game is subpar and not viable without expansions.
Thus we're left with an optimisation puzzle where over time a dominant strategy will emerge, and only the luck of the draw shakes things up a bit.

Furthermore the game lacks any meaningful or direct player interaction.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A great game for people who like games of the cube pushing/resource management variety with a dose of "deck-building" thrown in.

You've got a bag of workers, and each turn, you pull some of them from the bag and allocate them to different tasks. Most tasks will move you up a track, making you better at something, while also adding another worker associated with that action to your bag. Your goal of course is to get a good bag of workers that give you high odds of pulling off your chosen strategy. Points are scored in a multitude of ways, so you have to choose which way is most efficient for you.

While nothing in the game is complicated, there's a lot of it to absorb, so a really nice game in the medium-heavy euro vein.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
One of this year's pouch-building games (the theme of the show, I am told), Orleans was a surprising disappointment. I hadn't read the rules beforehand, but had heard positive buzz. The game itself was much lighter than I would prefer, and mostly involved spending actions to collect points in a rather straightforward way. The engine-building consisted almost exclusively of getting more actions, so that you can collect more points per round. One of the other players felt there were no meaningful decisions in the game. While that's a little farther than I would go, I didn't think the game gave me any reason to feel invested in my decisions. 

The game's thematic trappings actually hindered my enjoyment further. The game contains a number of different currencies, each of which is just a value of points. For example, there is money that you can't spend (each dollar is one point). There are goods that you collect but don't use or consume (a wheat is one point, a cheese is two, etc.). They might as well have used a single currency - VP - and just printed a value on every token. It would have at least saved me the burden of having to check the reference board all the time in order to figure out whether wine or yarn is more valuable.

It's not terrible, but I'd be okay never playing this again. There are too many better games out there.

RATING: 7
Based on 1 play with 2 players:

I LOVE the pacing of this game. I love that the rulebook calls out the steps that players can theoretically do simultaneously, and can just call for turn order if it's going to matter. Our group has a habit of finding these steps in games and doing things this way anyway, but serious props for actually calling it out and making it official. Even for us, I think it helped us just jump in right away and get to it.

I like this game more as a Euro than as a deckbuilder. The deckbuilding just feels like a nice way to add some variety and keep you on your toes, rather than being an interesting aspect unto itself here. Largely because the game requires diversity, whereas in most deckbuilders the goal is to create a refined group of specific synergies. In this it seems like at most you want to grab everyone you can, it's just a matter of when and how to do it efficiently (where you maybe prune out one follower type). 

So while it's not exactly what I look for in a deckbuilder, I do think it folds into the Euro formula nicely. Adding "luck of the draw" to a Euro may make the game sound a little reactionary, and it is. There's ways to mitigate your luck of course, but you're not going to be totally screwed if you fly by the seat of your pants. I kind of enjoy that. It gives the game a relaxed feel.

Only tried at 2p so far, and I can see where 3-4p might be more interesting and send you down different paths more often, but honestly I thought the game was good enough at 2p that while I'd happily try it with more, I'm not really champing at the bit to get it to the group.

Also, as with all games that have a static setup I do worry about replayability - specifically that there might be an optimal route with the place tiles. There's an optional rule that suggests letting players draft some out before play, though I think it might be more interesting to remove a few at random instead. Will experiment.

RATING: 7
A solitaire-ish, puzzle-y game in the new-school euro style. But I felt it was simple enough and streamlined enough that I quite enjoyed it. I liked it more than many other games of its type. 

I thought the timing and competition on the shared boards are enough to inject some variability and adaptability (and some pleasant viciousness) into the game. 

I like that the game allows you to set up your actions independently, relying on the honor system to make sure no one is spying on other people's boards for advantage. The quick playing and low downtime nature of the game (while still maintaining some interaction)is one of its biggest draws. 

Also, I love that when you get resources, you don't do anything with them. There is no ridiculous conversion engine you have to deal with. Just points. Refreshing.

RATING: 8
This game is very, very good! I had heard a lot of my friends praise this game, so my expectations were high – and yet they were fulfilled! So far I have only tried it a couple of times, both with 4 players, and I would love to try it with 2 and 3 as well. This is a bag building eurogame with a boring theme (not that I mind) and a few ways to score points - I honestly wouldn’t call it a point salad game as some people do as you only score points for money and goods and buildings at the end of the game… There aren’t a whole lot of different actions, but due to the plethora of buildings you can try different strategies each time (although there are only a few ways to score points, there are still a handful of different strategies).

Of course there is luck – you draw chits randomly from a bag, and I have witnessed several bad draws, but generally it didn’t seem to be that critical, although it sucks so much when you just don’t get the guy you need... What was critical, however, was how you could lose an early guy to a plague at random… This gave the players who didn’t get hurt a huge advantage as you basically wasted your entire first turn when you claimed the guy in the first place! That is an unnecessary dose of luck that I don’t like. Also, unlike other deck and bag building games, you might not be able to cycle through you “deck”, so you might not ever see a particular chit, which can hurt you a lot… Sure, you can send some chits away so that you “clean” your bag a bit and cycle through your bag faster, but I’m still not sure I like this.

What I did like was the ease of learning and small amount of interaction - although to be fair there is probably more than I give credit for. Because you really need to watch out where the other players are going on the map so you don’t get stuck as building guildhalls is a huge source of points! It also matters a lot when sending your workers away to the common board as you can get a great reward if your timing is right (in both of my plays I actually timed it great as I could close one of the areas and therefore claim the citizen token, but I just didn’t draw the token I needed and had several of…)

I am very impressed with this game and think that it’s a great, light/medium weight euro! If only it wasn’t so expensive I would probably buy it, and I might end up doing so anyway as I think my wife will enjoy it a lot as you’re mostly minding your own business, although you do need to watch out for the different tracks and where the other players build guildhalls. I highly recommend this game!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Great game. You build a bag of workers and the combination you pull on your turn determines what actions you can take. They game is very free in how you utilize your workers as you could take no actions and solely set up for the next turn with a bad draw, but then you have a great turn coming. It is neat that used workers always go back into the bag so it's not like you can cycle your bag and get that wild worker, you just need to get lucky on the pull or manage your actions so you leave less guys in the bag. There is so much to do and not enough time, and with 18 game rounds, you definitely have the space to see your strategy in action. There is a nice blend of tactics (responding to other players in the various races that are going on) and strategy (what actions get upgraded with technology and what extra action spaces you build) that give this game a lot of perceived depth. Love it and hope to own it soon.

RATING: 4
Not by any stretch of the imagination a terrible game, but one that's not really for me, I'm afraid. Bag-building games are essentially deckbuilders where the deck is shuffled every round, and while it's better than a lot of the drecky post-Dominion deckbuilders (looking your way Star Realms and Ascension), I miss the frisson of discovery that comes along with a new and unexpected combination of cards. Mechanically, there are some aspects that remind me of De Vulgari Eloquentia and Village but I end up missing those games' quirky emergent narratives. And the whole package reminds me of another low-theme Euro puzzler set in Ye Olden Days France, Troyes, but even Troyes has more player interaction than this one; apart from some mildly interesting timing races and a tiny dash of area control in the race to place guild halls on the map, this one has nada.

Long story short, yet another game that can't escape the shadow of other better games for me.

** WARNING: SERIOUS GAME-NERD BUZZWORD BUKKAKE AHEAD**

Obviously, lots of people disagree with me on this point. Some people can't get enough of this style of attractive-looking ultra-low conflict games (even I am occasionally charmed by them) but to me the games that really end up standing out from the crowd are the ones that are playgrounds for emergent behavior and unexpected synergies. My distinct impression of Orleans is that the whole system lies on the surface level and none of the subsystems (which are impeccably well-designed) actually interact in a way that causes you to be surprised and delighted in the night. Despite a plethora of point sources (many commodities plus the inevitable cardboard deniers AND IT IS ALWAYS DENIERS), all of the things you get for doing the things you do in the game are just, in the end, points. The game rewards the person who finds the most efficient path to vacuuming up points in exactly the way the designers envisioned it. If that's fun for you, great. It doesn't spark joy for me.

RATING: 7
EDIT 31.05.2015 
Having played it more, I feel I have been a bit harsh in my rating, probably due to the high expectations I had for this game. It is indeed a fine game, rich with theme and novel mechanisms, intertwined quite well. I am getting used to the spacial element of the game, now it does not feel foreign to the design. Still, I feel the game could have been better:

- there is too much luck in the bag pulling mechanism, as is, which given the scope and duration of the game feels out of place. I would prefer to play it dominion-style, where you have to go through all your followers before you refill the bag

- the place tiles seem too few to start with, and a couple of them are still quite strong, almost too strong (school, laboratory and windmill) especially in combos with each other or by placing technology tiles on them

- the events seem unnecessary and uninspired. Should have been removed from the game

- there seems to be a dominant strategy, that if not stopped will win the game. It involves working towards completing the development track, and halfway through the game switching towards building houses, all while collecting as much money as possible as a secondary objective.

- the boatman track seems less useful than the rest

Overall, a very good game that deserves its nomination for KedJ 2015. Upped the rating from 6 to 7.

-----

Engine building euro, with a bag building mechanism. Although the main concept is appealing, the game felt underdeveloped and rushed in many ways:

- the council sub-game devolves into a game of chicken, where you go to thin your deck and even get paid for it
- the money seems underutilized as a currency, used mainly as VP and sometimes for taxes
- the spatial subgame, although perhaps thematic, feels disjointed from the engine building nature of the game
- the goods are just diffent VPs, nothing more
- several buildings seem terribly strong, others completely underwhelming (1 joker-monk for 2 VPs??)

There are even more nitpicks, but i will stop here. The other main problem is that turn order at times is crucial to the action that you want to take due to resource scarcity, but there is hardly anything you can do to influence it. Even worse, pulling the wrong chits out of the bag can mean the difference between a super round and a mediocre one. And unlike dominion and the like, the chits go back in the bag after you use them, so you cannot guarantee seeing your full "deck" in every rotation. Unless you thin to death, that is, which is mindnumbingly boring..

Overall, there is a solid game engine in the core of Orleans, and the art is fantastic, but the game was not polished enough and this shows. A missed opportunity.

RATING: 8
I'm as shocked as anyone that this one's here. Most folks know of my intense dislike of dice and/or randomness for the sake of randomness. So a worker placement game with different types of workers (ala Manhattan Project) that revolves entirely around drawing workers randomly from a bag didn't seem quite up my alley. I was very wrong. There's a lot of engine building and such early on that isn't affected by the random worker draws. Once you're set-up, the randomness adds a very tactical element where you must make decisions on the fly based on what you draw. It's brilliant, I love it.

RATING: 5
Not an altogether bad game, as I suspected from the Spiel preview when the game came out for the first time.The bag building captures the essence of deck building and especially deck thinning rather well: at some point you'll have more people in your bag than you can use effectively, so you'll have to work out some appropriate response to getting the whole in balance again.

That said, the game this idea is embedded in is bone dry and quite repetitive; and the artwork on the player boards not sufficiently contrast-rich compared to the tokens which will populate it. I was moderately enjoying myself upon playing it, but was also done with the whole after the game was finished. There isn't much to do save efficiently placing worker discs, for the rewards obtained don't really open up new vistas of the game's design. More bite wouldn't have been amiss.

RATING: 8.5
Very fun bag building game. This game is fairly easy to learn how to play despite having lots of components everywhere, and my wife really likes playing this one with me. 

I would like to note that we play with a house rule whenever we play this game: Instead of being able to take any building you want, we reveal 4 buildings from the level one and level two stack each and those are the buildings available to purchase. After a building is purchased, you put another one out from the deck. People tend to do the same old strategies again and again if given free reign of the buildings, and we felt this additional rule forced us to change things up with every game. This rule is also very beginner friendly, as those with analysis paralysis problems tend to look at each and every tile before finally just picking one.

RATING: 9.7
It does look busy but this is just Bruges plus in weight. 3.0 on a five point scale is accurate. It plays slightly longer than Bruges. I enjoyed this midweight game. Cara won 128 to 109. My church to avoid events seemed mildly beneficial and Cara's bathhouse seemed wildly beneficial so that helped her. Also she did collect a lot of goods using the point to point movement option. I did promote a few more people to the "special deeds to work on" board, but overall it was not enough. I believe I collected too many monks (which are wild tokens in this game). It feels like Augustus mashed up with a Feld game, but it is a Civ/general board game/modern Bruges style board game. It fills a similar niche and we can never have too many of these kinds as right now the main ones that spring to mind are just 3 titles: Bruges, Rococo, and Orleans. All of these have been recently made. All are squarely medium in weight, can fairly easily get played, and feel like "main event" games which are really "nice" and slickly modern compared to the more generic and popular titles of the same weight (some of which help to fill BGG's top 200 list). All of them compare well with heavier weight titles that don't get pulled out as much.

Still. I wish Siberia played with two better. I liked it too but I don't think it really works too well with two players. Orleans, of course, works fine with two.

Start out with Augustus and Bruges. Then play Orleans maybe. It might make it make more sense.

Cara wins our 2nd round. I don't demand anything more than this to be fully satisfied. That is a real nice game there.

RATING: 3
Played this a couple of times with 4 very experienced gamers - the first time we were learning the mechanisms and game flow and despite some misgivings we tried again. Sadly we didn't even finish as it was such a painful experience. Negative chits early on made the game tough and then those who were unlucky with chit draws suffered badly. After a long discussion and analysis we decided this was too fiddly a game with little variation in play styles available. Not sure how this got into production - there are so many much cleaner and better designed games out there (even some I don't like) but this one I won't play again and can see little merit in the design..

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Deck-building meets worker placement. Well, it's more "drawing pool in the bag"-building than deck-building, really. With a deck of cards you cycle through the deck, or you choose the cards you want to use during a turn. In Orléans you have to draw the "cards" from a bag, and trust your luck. Theoretically it's also possible that a "card" you've put in the bag never gets drawn. The workers are used to gain more workers (drawing pool in the bag-building) or perform an action (moving on the central game board and build houses). The type of worker you gain also has an immediate impact (gain money or goods, advance on the literacy track...). Nice game, but some of the tiles give too big an advantage to the owner. Furthermore, as far as thematic depth is concerned, we are in very shallow waters here. The game does not give you the feeling you are in medieval France.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Wow. This game blew me away. At the heart of it, it is not a very complex game. You send your worker out to different locations to gain new workers and new abilities, including technology tiles that make it easier to complete tasks, drawing more tiles per round, free resources, or buildings that allow you to do special actions that your opponents cannot. You also want to travel around the board to build trading posts or draw more tiles. However, this is a deeply strategic game with numerous paths to victory. Highly enjoyable and replayable.

[17 Jul 2017] - 7.0 (1 play)
I have avoided playing this game just based on the theme and art alone. When I finally did play this game, it wasn't so bad. It's a cross between worker placement and deck building in that you pull workers from a bag each round and then place them. That means you need to be thinking about thinning out workers that are no longer needed and make sure you can cycle through those remaining quick enough. From there it's a basic worker placement, resource gathering euro with a random event every round.

RATING: 8
Gets high marks for being the first (or second) of its kind and for being fun to play, but some oddities about the game suggest that it underwent quite a transformation during development, leaving tonsils-like vestiges of earlier versions. For one thing, two items (grain and money) have the same value; only one needs to be in the game. For another, the rules devote a good chunk of space to penalties that are unlikely ever to be imposed. Also, playing on the Beneficial Deeds board never seems to be an optimal play, at least in a two-player game. Maybe this will all make sense when the expansions come.

[2015-02-14 First Impression]
Lots of fun. Orleans plays much different from what I expected after reading the rules. Every turn is a nice little puzzle based on the pieces you draw, the special actions you manage to aquire and the strategy you try to pursue.

:star: Not so hard to teach. This is a rather straight forward game that should be easy to teach to people that have played a deck builder before.
:star: The events spice things up a little. In the game I played, the tax events appeared rather late and so the hoarding of resources was expensive. This might be another thing to keep in mind in the next game.
:star: I`m not so sure if there are really multiple routes to victory or if this is more of a race where everyone is aiming for the same things and you`re just trying to get the best scoring tiles first. Either way, currently I'm okay with it either way.
:star: Our first game was quite long. It might be that we're slow players, but we weren't even close to 90 minutes.

RATING: 9
Wonderful bag builder, themed on the Loire Valley in medieval times.  Players draw workers from a bag, and place them on their personal action board.  Actions that are completed can be activated, which advances players in the game, and grants more workers of specific types.  Any actions triggered, and workers earned, return those workers to the bag.  Effectively this is a deck building game in bag form.  Lovely pruning mechanic sending workers off to the town hall.

Initially busy game, but surprisingly fast and fluid to play.  Really liked it.  Much better than Hyperborea.

RATING: 9
Orleans. This game is flat out fun. Fine, fine, it's just collecting stuff and pushing cubes up your spread sheet, (what does cheese mean = cheese equals two points) but how it comes together is both clever and fun. There is also a map, I tend to have a soft spot for games which feature a map that is just one part of the game as it is here. The game is really simple, but at the same time there's some depth in the interlocking gears.  It reminds me of Goa in that sense, but with no auctions, and is more simple, simple enough to perhaps be a gateway plus perhaps.

The multiple ways of getting points, reminds me of five tribes (other point salads also but mostly five tribes), as does the way that the game starts so open with so many options, later the options are still there but most are not sensible. I feel like there is an analogy with life there...

Plays excellently with two. Is quite solitaire, but you have options to block people of optimal moves and parasite off their actions - but only on that shared board whatever that is called, which is my least favourite part of the game anyway as it feels a bit tacked on. There's a bit of a scramble to get monks also, which I find amusing in its athematic vibe. 

However, what makes this game for me, is that I haven't to date enjoyed a deck builder enough to keep it, mostly because I find repeatedly shuffling the cards fiddly and a bit boring, here you get all round that with the bag mechanic, which for me works so much better.

I think the artwork and overall aesthetic is gorgeous, that's the non-deluxe version straight out of the box.

 Recommended.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Played this game at BGGcon 2015. Really liked the spins on the worker placement mechanics. Each space takes several workers to activate and you will then in turn get more workers and advance on several different tracks to gain VP and so forth.

Light on theme, but I did enjoy the consistent medieval art style.

RATING: 8
a terrific mid-weight Euro in the true sense of a Euro.  The celebrated mechanic is “bag-building” which is similar to deck building but with a twist.  You collect character tokens which allow you to do actions, but you draw a limited number of these out of the bag each turn so you never really know what actions will be available to you each turn.  Then the aim is to manage the bag you are building so as to maximise the chance of being able to do the actions you want which allow you to optimise the strategy you are following.  Plenty of strategies to follow and the game plays smoothly.

This really is a crisp and dry Euro with a lot to think about.  This, along with Castles of Mad King Ludwig and Golden Ages are definitely my favourite Euros to come from Essen 2014.

RATING: 6
Take at least some of my comments with a grain of salt. Why? I have never actually played a normal game of Orleans. I've only played some of the scenarios from the Invasion expansion. This is a bag building game where you add tokens to a bag and draw them out. It's like a deckbuilder, but you are using a bag and tokens instead of cards. Also, deckbuilding is only a potion of what's going on in the game rather than the entire game. As far as my opinion, the underpinnings of this game are really solid when you consider the various competitive scenarios, solo scenarios, expansion modules, expanded place locations, and even a coop scenario. While the system itself is very flexible, the setup is extremely annoying. Depending on the number of players, you have to adjust many factors (number of tokens, goods, etc.) The randomization of the goods and placing them on the map is annoying. Especially, when you consider that it's easy to miss a spot or accidentally filling in a spot for 3/4 players when playing with less. The game itself has a a few other issues that I dislike: The goods/money *mainly* serve the same function, which is being worth a specific amount of VP at the end of the game. In addition, the events become kind of boring and trivial. These last two points can be alleviated via the Trade & Intrigue expansion and the new trade beneficial deeds board is supposed to be much better. In the end, this is a good game and I can see it's appeal, but it's also not one I'm jonesing to keep in my collection.

RATING: 9
A beautiful union of two great mechanics. I love that players are not simply influenced by which characters they want or need in their bag, but also by the associated bonus attributed with acquiring said workers. Not only that, but as in most deck builders more workers isn't always better, and players must balance character acquisition (and associated bonuses) with acquiring victory points. The game ramps up very nicely, and I always leave the game feeling satisfied. My main qualm is the severe lack of variability. The game is wide open, and players can pursue any strategy they want in any given game, but the game does not encourage adaptive play in the slightest. Unless house-ruled players can rinse-and-repeat strategies too easily.

RATING: 4
[b]The bag-building sounds interesting on the surface, but by the end of my second play, this revealed itself to me as a salad roll - rice-paper thin theme with a helping of point-salad. Runs too long for what it is.

Wife's opinion: Loved first play. At end of her second play, she felt it was already getting samey.

Rating history: 8|6|4[/b]

[b]After 3 plays...[/b] ORLEANS ended up being a bit of a disappointment. The first play was fun enough (I tend to be a sucker for novelty), but half-way through my second play, I felt like I was essentially doing the same thing all over again. I get that there is a sandbox element to the game, and I need to try new things, but I don't find the different ways to pursue points to be that interesting (I usually find this a problem with point-salad games).

Maybe it's the 2p game that does work as well for me, but the more I think about it, the more I think that it's the gameplay itself. I like analyzing the map and racing for the high-quality points, but Broom Service does that better as a main part of gameplay, and nothing else in Orleans really grabs me.

RATING: 9.5
TMG did a fantastic job of reprinting this game. All components are thick and sturdy. And the velvety bags are beautiful. No insert though, but my usual plastic container solution does the trick. As far as gameplay, I love it. Very smooth, turns go quick, so many directions you can go in. The obvious travel strategy seems a little overpowered. But with more plays, I want to experiment the money strategy. On my most recent play, I actually won by a narrow margin because of money, despite being behind in everything else. My only complaint--besides what I just mentioned--would be the 18 events. Some are rewarding, but those that cause you to pay (e.g. goods, money, etc.) seem too easy. My wife and I always had ample money or goods to pay when required. And losing a character to a plague was never a huge loss as we can generally just get the same one back on the next turn. Otherwise, excellent game!

RATING: 6
I like the idea behind Orléans. The way bag-building is used here reasonably simplifies its deck-building roots and still allows for meaningful differences to develop between players. At least in theory.

In practice, the game doesn't quite succeed. it is too limited in strategical scope. For example, soldiers and machines are must-haves for anyone early on. Only after they have been exhausted can players actually start to meaningfully move away from the pack. It's a pity that the game cannot quite meet its ambitious goals because it feels really promising.

The only other detractors are mostly incidental: The rulebook is so full of holes and ambiguous rules it's not funny, and the chits start showing wear pretty quickly which is a little unfortunate. Maybe cubes or meeples would have been a better choice for the grab bag content.

RATING: 6
The game is fine but is way too overrated on BGG.

Pretty standard Euro with bland/empty theme and much room for mechanical improvement (ie player interaction and interchange in value of goods collected/market).... being outdone already by many other games such as on kickstarter.  This is also a case where I think having open information kills a bit of the fun; you know what everyone is planning to do, but there is not a ton you can do to change others' decisions, and you cannot do much to change your own planned actions either.

Nevertheless, bag-building is fun, and there is this unique layer of strategy trying to get your tiles to match-up and building your bag appropriately.  Game also gets more tense towards the end.

RATING: 7
Glad I got to try this! The primary mechanism of building a bag of workers, buying those workers to move along effect tracks, and then pulling those workers blindly to activate spaces worked quite well together and make for an intriguing game. Easy to understand and play but not too easy to do everything you want. You have to respond repeatedly to bad luck, either from the events or from the worker pull. I typically like those sorts of games. I'm a little concerned about the replayability, as I did find activate my personal board actions quite dry and I can't imagine feeling inspired by doing it again (and it is what basically drives the game) but I was thinking about what I could have done differently for a next game, so that's a good sign! So, a very nice game that I enjoyed quite a bit, but not one I feel like is a signature game and that I would want immediately in my collection. But I would definitely play it again!

RATING: 8.7

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I have mixed feelings on this game. It's clearly got a fresh design with a mechanic you might call action building, which I would describe as a hybrid version of action selection, set collecting, and deck building. In that regard the game works, and is interesting. You try to puzzle out what tokens you have for the round vs which actions you most want to take, and whether you'll settle for something inefficient in terms of actions, but effective at scoring points. In addition, you can build up your engine in different ways, and that has a wonderful feel to it.

However, on the down side, it feels under-developed. Interaction is low, except for a furious race to build the best buildings, which feel poorly balanced. The engine burns out before the game is over, so you're stuck playing somewhere between a few, and a half dozen turns on auto-pilot. Actions get eliminated as you progress, and you can reach the end of tracks as well. The scoring is exceptionally bland, with goods and money being simply VPs, with virtually no reason to distinguish between them. It leaves you wondering why this aspect wasn't developed further, as there could have easily been a meaningful layer there that was left behind for no good reason. After a few buildings are built, it feels more and more solitaire, and less and less like you're making difficult decisions. Kind of a let down in that regard.

Not a bad game by any stretch, and I prefer this to Hyperborea, which was a more ambitious, but IMO less successful design using this new and novel mechanic. I get why people are fawning over this design, but I think it will either need to be tweaked with many house rules or will not hold up to repeated plays. We'll see as I'm interested enough to give it another go or two.

Update - increased rating after a 5p game. Seems to play well at 5. Not sure it's as well balanced for less than that.

Update 2 - game is growing on me. Up to about 6 plays now. Some of the balance issues I was concerned about early had more to do with how players were playing the game.

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5
It's a pure bag builder, by which I mean that it doesn't use a discard pile to emulate a deck builder and instead uses the fact that it doesn't need one. I'm of two minds about this: I don't like that you never cycle your bag, meaning that there's always a chance to never draw a specific character you went out of your way to buy. I do like the simplicity and that it further incentivizes you to thin out your bag.

The game itself is simple euro goodness. There's many different ways to get points and specialize your engine and there's some slight competition for the limited resources. The turns are fast and the game has a nice flow to it.

I don't like the vague secret but simultaneous and non-hidden placement, but that's easily house ruled.

RATING: 9
Fun worker placement game with a twist: there are different types of worker and to perform each action you need to allocate the correct workers to the action space. The snag is that you draw your workers randomly from a bag, so there's no guarantee you'll get the workers you need. It's all about adapting your plan to the best option each turn, plus planning ahead by acquiring the right workers, or the Monks which can serve as a wild worker. I like this game a lot.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4.5
I don't like point hoarding game, they are very boring and repetitive. Especially if there is nothing else to do like in this game. The points means feels like just numbers in a bank account, I don't get any pride or joy or agricola-like feeling of getting those points. I don't even know why the money is in that game... I think it should be removed and just be more points (yay more points), not to mention the production and shipping price a tiny bit.. With taxes, you lose points. That would reduce the amount of components and accelerate as well as simplify it a little. Point hoarding is so uncreative. 

Everything in the game feels bland unlike agricola which is gratifying to build and buy things. In this game, everything is just points. Buidling stuff only gets you more points, if you do another thing it makes you more points, etc. For me, the game sucks.

Not because I think the game sucks that there is nothing good in it. The token building mechanic is good, and would have been excellent in a game when what you do meant more than just making victory points.

The travelling map is somewhat interesting... but again it's just some point hoarding. If the map would have been some trading area where you built caravans to create production chains that would give you money which would allow you to improve your company, trade routes or Orléans itself, that would have made the map more interesting.

I don't see the point of having that sideboard with the buildings, they do nothing, give no bonus or special ability, it should simply be removed from the game. It's just another crappy point hoarding thing... or when you build one of them, all players gets a certain benefit or the game generates more goods, or players receives an additionnal specific worker, etc.

There are plenty of things that this game should have been, but point hoarding ONLY is one of the worse it could be.

I strongly believe that there are several elements in this game that should be removed since I don't see their use and they absolutely bring nothing more to the game (like the sideboard with the buildings or the coins). This game feels very unidimensional and it is quite superficial (in the sense of no depth).

If I take another point based game like "Agricola" you have a feeling of building something, of surviving, or buying and trading... in Orléans, there is really no feeling that you are playing a trader at all, it feels more like you are gatering bread crumps and whomever has the most wins... boring. In "The Village", you have that feeling that time is running out and that having a plan B if someone ruins your plan A or that you are putting sticks into the wheels of your opponents by activating the market first, etc. You don't have any of those feelings in Orléans.

Orléans is a game that could have been much more and for me it fell quite short of its potential. It is a very tasteless, feelingless game for me.

I think the game has not been playtested enough, but especially with lots of different kind of players.

RATING: 9
I always enjoy playing Orleans because of how it combines resource management with worker placement with its unique bag-building mechanism. I love the challenge of managing not only which workers my bag is composed of, but the round-to-round balancing act between what's in the bag and what's on the action board. 

It's also a great next-step game to teach to new players. When set up it can look very challenging and complicated, but every group I've ever taught it to has been able to grasp very quickly how the game works and it's fun to watch new players' eyes light up as they begin to see the various paths they can explore in the game.

RATING: 3
The game uses a "worker-pool building" system in which the players acquire worker tokens and draw them from a bag to allocate to various action spaces on their personal boards. Each action requires a specific set of workers, and that is what drives the game. Unfortunately, the actions are uninteresting and boring.

This is one of those bloated designs that give you lots of choices to give an impression of depth. There are many tracks for your cubes to advance, a dozen or more unique buildings to choose from, various goods that are simply different point values, a map where your figure moves around via roads and rivers to pick up those goods and to build houses, and so on. There are even events (the trademark of a designer who doesn't know when to stop) that are completely... uneventful. Also, the balance seems a little off, especially with some buildings.

To be fair, mechanically the game works. But it's ultimately an unengaging and dry exercise in engine building that is not helped by the unappealing artwork.

RATING: 7
(12/25/2015) Comment based on three plays.

This is an interesting medieval-themeed game centered around a unique action-token pull mechanism. Each player's tableau has a number of action spaces that are triggered only when two or three specific combinations of action-tokens are assigned to them. In a matter of speaking, this is a deck building game, where tokens are used instead of cards, and the deck is reshuffled with both new and old cards at the end of every turn.

There are seven types of tokens representing the various characters of Orleans: monks, farmers, craftsmen, merchants, sailors, soldiers, and scholars. Starting with only four tokens, players assign them to action spaces that beget new tokens, advancement on progress tracks, gold, buildings, and/or automation. The new tokens and tokens used to trigger the action are thrown back into a bag, where a new set of tokens are drawn for the next turn. The token pull mechanism is similar to the one in [thing=35285][/thing], where turn order is determined by the pull. In Orleans, turn order is clockwise, but the actions you can take will be determined by what tokens are pulled and how you assign them.

There is a lot of tension and frustration as players pull and assign tokens, because a bad pull can result in fewer or no actions. As more tokens are generated and thrown into the bad, pulling specific combinations of characters to trigger specific actions becomes increasingly difficult. Generating solider tokens also allows a player to pull progressively more tokens, starting from four all the way to eight tokens. Also, monks can be powerful as they act as wild cards for most action spaces. Lastly, tokens can be permanently reassigned off the player tableau for bonuses, similar to "thinning the deck", so to speak. Because of the token pull, there is a substantial element of luck, but given the above mentioned choices, this can be somewhat mitigated. Thus, players must be flexible and adaptable to the ever changing results of the token pull; there is no single clear path to victory.

Instead, there seems to be many, many paths to victory, like the many branches of a tree. At first glance, this feels like a Feld "point salad", where almost every action results in some generation of VP in terms of gold or progress in some track, but it becomes clear with a few plays that there are some key strategies. However, with the varying order of event tiles, and random distribution of goods on the travel board, newer strategies will probably evolve with more plays.

The kickstarter edition that replaces the cardboard chits with wooden, stickered tokens is gorgeous and tactile-wise very satisfying. The artwork is detailed, but not distracting. Play is quick, with very little downtime, even with the full complement of four players. Mechanics-wise, it is easy to start playing. Still, the game has a somewhat higher learning curve to play well. The game is not very forgiving, where a small misstep early on will be magnified into unsatisfying play and poor scores. It is all about maximizing the Euro-efficiency of one's engine, but in terms of maximizing the probabilities. I think it is an intriguing deck builder that "fools" you into thinking it is something novel with the use of token pulls. Still, I'm not exactly excited to play a friend's copy any further, as beyond the token pulls, it is a multi-variate pissing contest. Not sure I care much for that, but then again, I didn't care much for the Feldian point salad at first. Time will tell if this design grows on me. Initial rating is a "7".

RATING: 8

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.5
A bag-building game. Each round you draw your allotted amount of tokens out of the bag and place them on your board to do actions. Most actions require 2-3 particular colored tokens to activate, but there are gears you can acquire which permanently makes the affected action need one less token (you cover the token you want to ignore with the gear) and there are some additional actions (buildings) you can add to your board that might only use 1 token.

For the first half of the game, timing is rarely an issue so very often, we execute all actions one player at a time (in turn order). This helps to speed the game up. When it does matter (generally near the end of the game when resources are running out or multiple people are sending tokens to the town hall), then we do the one-action-per-player-at-a-time as per the rules.

RATING: 7.8
Bokkensprong Sinaai 17/2/2017. De startspeler draait een gebeurtenistegel om die aan het einde van de ronde zal worden uitgevoerd, en wie eerst en laatst op het boerenspoor staat, krijgt een punt meer resp. minder. Dan trekt elke speler zijn maximaal aantal personages (wit boer, zwart handelaar, geel monnik, grijs professor, rood ridder, bruin ambachtsman) uit zijn trekzak en plaatst die op zijn actiebord (iedereen terzelfder tijd), waarna elke speler om beurt een actie mag uitvoeren tot niemand er nog heeft. Elke gewenste actie vergt een specifieke combinatie van personages (vb. rood, wit en blauw). Acties laten toe meer personages te krijgen, grondstoffen (=punten) te bekomen, extra actievelden te krijgen, vooruit te gaan op het kennisspoor, personages weg te schenken in ruil voor geld of kennis, te reizen over land of water op de landkaart, huizen te bouwen in bezochte dorpjes of in Orléans zelf, etc. Hier en daar vallen burgers te verdienen, die worden bijgeteld bij het aantal huizen en daarna worden vermenigvuldigd met het aantal sterren op het kennisspoor (=punten). Wanneer een actie wordt uitgevoerd, worden de personagefiches terug in de trekzak gestopt. Ten slotte wordt de gebeurtenis uitgevoerd (soms vb. extra inkomsten, soms vb. belastingen). Goederen en geld leveren nog punten op aan het eind.
Strategisch spel met redelijke geluksfactor door het willekeurig trekken van personagefiches uit de zak. Het eerste spel heeft wel veel te lang geduurd doordat iedereen te lang nadacht bij het plaatsen van personagefiches (3u...).

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
I liked this one a lot, sort of like Concordia in that it's a family-weight game but it's also interesting enough for people used to more complex games. The core of the game is solid, you're collecting tokens that represent different people, and the actions you can take on your turn require specific combinations of people. The bag-building mechanic is pretty similar to deck-building, but the way this one works you're more just looking to balance the ratios of your tokens so you have a variety of good options than you are hoping to get particular combos of cards like in most deck-builders. This plus the fact that there are wild guys makes me feel more in control than in a typical deck-builder. But the really genius thing is how most of the tokens also come with a bonus like unlocking a new actions spot just for you, making other actions easier to activate later, or just points. Balancing getting the right ratios of different guys in your bag while also collecting the bonuses you want is the central conflict you have to deal with. And there's a really cool system for culling your bag while getting some nice benefits, so you do have a lot of control of what's in your bag.

RATING: 7
Orléans is a combination of worker placement and pool building (in this case, building a bag of tokens). It has plenty of Euro-style goodness with multiple paths to winning and a heavy emphasis on players racing each other to achieve or claim certain positions. 

There are some aspects of the game that detract from the expeirence. The pool building is a bit funky, with it being less of an engine-building affair than other such games. Luck of the draw can be mitigated, but sometimes it shows up even when kept at bay. And while I'm glad the game does have at least some player interaction, it is a shame that requires slowing the game down to have players perform actions one-at-a-time even when the chance for interaction is minimal.

That said, it's still an enjoyable Euro for fans of the two core mechanics.

RATING: 8
So there’s barely any theme and the game has some fiddly bits. You know what? In Orléans these things don’t bring it down. They don’t lift it up either, but that’s not necessary since the underlying game is more than solid. Assign your workers, build your bag, and improve your engine. It all feeds into itself which makes successfully managing all these parts and making them synergise into a point-scoring machine feels really rewarding. Even if you don’t quite pull that off, you can certainly look back at the end of a game and see that you’ve built something nice, even if it wasn’t quite enough to land you a win.

RATING: 8
2015-12-31

Initial Rating: 7.0 (December 2015)

I’m a bit disappointed. Orleans is a good game and I enjoy it, but due to the hype, I was expecting something more. Still, I want to play it again and it has a permanent spot in my game collection, but I thought Orleans was going to surpass Hyperborea for me, and it didn’t. 

The game components are very good. The art is also good, but its style isn’t for me. The theme is boring (to me) but I don’t mind because it is a good Euro game.

I love deck builder games, but the constant shuffling is a hassle, I also like dice building games but they have too much uncontrollable luck, so the perfect solution for me are the bag building games. I was expecting to enjoy Orleans more than I enjoyed Hyperborea but that wasn’t the case. I’m not sure which game came first so I’m not sure which game came up with the bag building innovation, but I hope more bag building games pop up soon. I will buy them all.

The rules aren’t complex. You can explain them under 20 minutes and the gameplay is around 2 hours. When you read that the game takes 18 turns you feel the game is very long, but the turns flow quickly and at the end you wish you had a couple more turns to accomplish more.

The mechanism are very similar to Hyperborea´s. You draw tiles form your bag and when the actions are completed they can be activated, the main difference is that when you do an action all the tiles return to the bag immediately. I’m not sure I like this as you are never assured to see all the tiles you acquired, it increases the luck factor and decreases the strategic factor (you can draw the same tile you already used before drawing some tiles you never used). To control this you can always leave some titles unused in your market but it feels fishy to me.

The game features lots of interesting decisions, namely which tiles to acquire (characters and buildings) but each decision is meaningful and has far reaching consequences. With each character you are tweaking the possibilities and capabilities of your “deck”. I LOVE this. With each building you are acquiring new abilities only you can use.

Some of the events have a very powerful effect. I dislike the one that makes you to lose one of your tiles because it is a random tile. You can really be screwed by this and the mitigation elements aren’t that many.

It seems the game has many paths to victory, but I feel the development track is a must if one wants to win, I’m not sure I like this, but you don’t need to be the leader on the development track, you need only to advance it to a good level so I’m ok with it. On the other hand it seems some of the actions/buildings are better than others, that is, I’m not sure about the balance of these actions/buildings. I need to play more times to see if this bothers me or not.

Bottom line, I need to play it again soon and I think I will buy all its expansions. As I said before, I was expecting a better game, but Orleans is a good game anyway and it has chances to get an improved rating from me. Hyperborea is a better game IMO. The gameflow is surprisingly quick.

Current Rating: 7.0

Also the random events can be devastating at the beginning of the game, but from midgame onward they seem (almost) meaningless.

2016-05-09

Between editions, the developers changed how a building works (Bathouse), however in the rulebook and on the tile itself, they left open the possibility of playing it in any way players want. I’m very bothered when publisher change a core rule between editions so I wanted to congratulate DLP games for doing it “right”. Leaving both options open is, definitely, the way to go :thumbsup:.

I hate when the second or third edition of a game change core rules. I have found Stonemaier Games (Viticulture, Euphoria) and Level99 Games (Pixel Tactics, War of Indines) to be particularly fond of this practice, and that has made me very wary of their games.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
First off, this game had a Feld-level of thematic wrapper (i.e. None). It's pretty much just point salad in a box.

Gameplaywise, it's got a lovely central mechanic which revolves around bag building. I love how you can see numerous strategic possibilities: the way that getting additional "workers" for your bag gives you a benefit at the time of collection - as well as an ongoing one - meaning you can easily clog up you bag just to get that initial benefit; The Townhall which allows you to thin out your bag AND score at the same time; The map which - being a great way to score VP - gets pretty contested and tense; the new Buildings which give you an ever growing action pool; the pretty simple resource system (in fact resource - coins, etc - are basically just VP).

However...

The pace of the game seemed a tad flat. You kind of just go through the motions for X rounds. I feel a big part of this are the event cards which should form the backbone for any arc but just seem really diluted. They just don't seem to have much of an impact on the game (at least based on my initial plays).

Overall, good but not great.  Will need to play some more...

RATING: 9.9
"Orleans" is one of my absolute favorite games of all time. The worker placement part is very intuitive and the "bag building" part feels pretty unique to me.
Down time is very low for a strategic game since the planning phase for each turn is done simlutanously and the actions can be done very quickly.
There is not a lot of player interaction, but racing to occupy specific spaces, buy unique building cards and reach goals for rewards on the public boards is enough interaction for me in a strategic game like this. In these aspects lie quite a few different strategies.
I always find it hillarious to pull the workers from the bag and getting rewarded for taking risks (or punished and try to make the best out of it).
This game got even better with particular modules from the expansions, adding variety to the random events occuring each turn and the public board ("Trade & Intrigue") as well as a very solid coop-mode and 2 solo scenarios ("Invasion"),
The game works for me with all player counts from 1-4 (even though the number of solo-scenarios is very limited) and I can teach the complete game to experienced players in about 15-20 minutes.
The game is not thematic, which doesn't bother me personally in this case because the mechanics are interesting enough to me. Only the set up tends to be a little bit annoying since there is a lot of different modules  and stuff to count and put in order for the different player counts and modes.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
For me this is a typical 'designer' game. Very fun and enjoyable to play a couple of times, but also a game that I quickly burned out on because of unilateral strategy. This game is a race in efficiency. Efficiency that sometimes gets frustrated by the luck of the draw, even with good risk-management. I think I can best describe this game as playing Dominion with the same set of cards (plus a few additions) over and over again. There are a couple of strategies but these are explored pretty fast. Then the only thing that remains with repeated plays is trying to optimize these strategies. But for me the game is not good enough to warrant these repeated plays. A few downsides I encountered: the balance of the bathhouse, very fast wear on tokens and an unsatisfying feeling about picking actions simultaneously to speed up game play when sometimes picking actions is dependent on persons before you. But picking actions in turn order will really slow down the game to the point of boredom.

RATING: 8
Rating based on about a dozen plays, the first few with the original game that had component wear issues; since then, I have played almost exclusively with the TMG edition that offers superior components. Only learning curve is knowing what the buildings due.  Tough to do well, you need to watch what your opponents are doing, be aware of the endgame scoring, plus have a good memory of what is in your bag, what may be surplus and timing issues when trying to earn Citizens and beat opponents to a city site in a crowded 4-5 P game.  Obviously there's alot of luck (a la Dominion) in getting the draws at the right time, but it's a fun and challenging game.  Wonder if it will see the expansions that RGG did with Dominion.  Right now, I'd have to rate it higher than Dominion because of the interaction with the map board as well as multiplicity of scoring options.
