--------------------------
Read rules Sept 2014

This game looks very promising! The central mechanic is a neat twist on some well established ideas. Each player has worker tokens which get drawn from their own bag each round. You can use them to perform actions, several of which will give you a new worker to put in your bag. You fight to collect resources and build trading posts on a shared map, and fight for once per game action spaces on a shared worker placement board. Each player also has a personal board for placing workers and can expand this board during the game.

The game also has a few punishing events for the players to deal with, costing them various resources. If you can't pay you select a form of torture. You know events a round in advance, so you see it coming, at least a little bit.

The game seems to be mostly resource management and action efficiency. It's hard to judge this one without playing but I'm optimistic. It's certainly a medium weight Euro. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
The buzzword of Essen 2014 was ‘bag-building’ and Orléans was one of the representatives. Given this novelty factor, I was surprised to recognize many details from Stockhausen’s previous release Siberia in Orléans. Both games feature the core challenge to cope best with what you’ve drawn. Both are about building up an engine enhancing future actions. In Siberia, the framework was more limited and tiles where drawn from one joint bag. It was also quicker and lighter. Orléans aims for a more full-fledged experience and targets strategy gamers.

On first impression, the engine-building part dominates the bag-building. The bag just happens to fill up with new tiles. When given the choice between the immediate function of a chosen action and the future value of the tile collected, the function mostly will win out. It’s an imperative to build up additional actions first, by getting soldiers for additional draws or at least by getting cog wheels reducing the needed input for actions. From there on, we tried to do the best with the bag content. At game end, trading points are the most valuable VP supplier, probably making any strategy unfeasible which neglects them completely. I got all except a single one of the most valuable good and still lost by a clear margin.

Hopefully, there’s more to Orléans than variations on one core strategy. I kept thinking afterwards about better combinations but for now I have a few reservations. 18 mostly repetitive rounds also took a bit too long to entice immediate replays.

RATING: 5
Two player too open, lacks player interaction. Event cards unbalanced as the game progresses. Clever concept let down by some glaring design flaws which almost seem to not have tested very well. 

Update after 4p play: Much improved over 2P yet still distinctly average overall. Certain decisions feel forced toward the end game as their are clear optimal moves, so the level of difficult and intriguing decisionmaking is sorely lacking. Feels like the game could be "solved" in a handful of plays. Still lacks meaningful player interaction and the scoring system is just stupid and feels like an afterthought. That said, it has a place as a lazy Sunday afternoon Euro if you really don't feel like thinking too hard, although setup and teardown is a chore. Still think this is the most overrated game in the BGG top 50.

RATING: N/A
During the medieval goings-on around Orléans, you must assemble a following of farmers, merchants, knights, monks, etc. to gain supremacy through trade, construction and science in medieval France.

In the city of Orléans and the area of the Loire, you can take trade trips to other cities to acquire coveted goods and build trading posts. You need followers and their abilities to expand your dominance by putting them to work as traders, builders, and scientists. Knights expand your scope of action and secure your mercantile expeditions. Craftsmen build trading stations and tools to facilitate work. Scholars make progress in science, and last but not least it cannot hurt to get active in monasteries since with monks on your side you are much less likely to fall prey to fate.

In Orléans, you will always want to take more actions than possible, and there are many paths to victory. The challenge is to combine all elements as best as possible with regard to your strategy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.3
This Kennerspiel des Jahres nominee is Reiner Stockhausen's 'bag building game', reusing some of his ideas of Siberia, still trying to make something novel. Well... I must say to me it did not feel like I was doing anything novel; this bag building (collecting workers in different colors for the worker placement phase where you can use them for different actions) felt just like dice pool building in Quarriors (or even Roll for the Galaxy for that matter) with the workers allowing less creativity than dice. Also it felt to me some game elements (e.g. buildings) were not fine-tuned; some of them felt strangely off the balance (yes, I say it only after one play but I played with some gamers who had played it quite a few times before and they agreed).
But that's all about complaints; otherwise it's a well-crafted complex Euro (well, rather JASEish but still), only nothing revolutionary. I even enjoyed my play of it, but would choose the above 4 complex Euros over it most of the time.

RATING: 10
05/2016 Orléans is a delightful eurogame in which you are a French noble who is using his influece to let the people in and around Orléans to do his bidding. During the game you allocate the workers in your pool to different location to acquire new workers and gain certain abilities. These new workers along with your starting workers are all put into a bag and each round you draw from this bag a certain number of workers, never knowing which ones. The first rounds go by quick but as your pool grows and the amount of actions you can do per round increases the game gets more and more interesting.

The game is great and there are a lot of different and interesting strategies to explore from game to game. Set up however takes a long time and the basic game comes with so-so components. The people in the game are represented by cardboard chits which can be upgraded with wooden meeples if you buy a "fankit" from the Publisher.

Still, I enjoy every game I play of this and Orléans will be part of my collection for a long time.

11/2016 Orléans has the honor to get my first ever perfect 10 rating. It is a fantastic game that I could play anytime. With each and every play, I still discover new and interesting strategic avenues to persue. I just bought the first expansion and I can't wait to see what this adds to the gameplay.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Orleans can be best described as a bag builder: A bit like the mechanism in Yggdrasil, but much more directed like a traditional deck builder. You're pulling chits out of the bag, and placing them on your board to get actions and other chits. Eventually you'll be traveling around the map collecting resources and going up in different tracks.

Apart from the bag-building aspect, it's a nothing-special medium-weight euro. The bag-building aspect is what makes it most unique. However, even forgetting about the bag-building aspect, it does offer a variety of strategies to score points (which in our game were suspiciously well-balanced since we all ended within a point of each other).

One big word of warning: The starting chits were extremely worn on the last day of the con. So much so that I'd hate to be playing with them (almost hard to recognize the picture because so much of it had rubbed off). They clearly need to fix those production issues. Someone also recommended using some kind of clear spray that they use on wargaming chits.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.25
I would really like to give this game a better rating, but I can't. I am sorry. There are just too many little things that add up for me on the negative side, to let me give a ranking in the 8's or 9's like so many of you (plus known reviewers!) have. Let me sum up these negative thoughts real quick:

1. Too less player interaction. Yes, you can block other folks' houses on the mutual map, but choosing other paths is always an option and you most likely won't block anyone at all, not even in a four player game. Also in the first x turns it is completely unimportant what everyone else is doing, you can focus on your own strategy entirely and have no downside whatswhoever, guaranteed. Not until the last seven to eight rounds you will have to wait for the others to take their actions first to really profit from their decisions and executing better short term tactics. This is fine, but a little few for me. Hyperborea for instance does it better with direct player combat (e.g. killing other peoples characters), cards that let you destroy something from nearby players (e.g. VP or cubes) and cards that let you copy the abilities of other players cards. All of this is non-existent in Orleans, even if it would have been easy to implement (e.g. a tile for destroying other folks' buildings/tokens, a tile for copying the ability of another players tile etc.). 

2. Being first player becomes very important in the later states (especially considering the spots on your dying people) and so the game is not really balanced on who is becoming the first player in e.g. round 10, a point where being first in turn order can win you the game. So, some crazy person could calculate this ten rounds back and decide from that point on who sould be the first player on round 1, just to be in charge when it gets important, which is sad. There should have been another action on every playerboard(!) to become first player in the next round. Maybe just with 1 or 2 tokens to activate it, so that the penalty for the losing part of two players both choosing it in the same round is not too high. You get my general idea here.

3. Balance is still an issue. For instance Brown clearly is too strong in the beginning. I have never seen any player win, that did not go 2-3 times on Village and choosing brown tokens in the first 1-4 rounds.  The gearwheels are just to powerful in the beginning and allow for a much smoother and efficient engine than e.g. a fast priest (Yellow) stategy. I have seen people try fast priest (including myself) and it mostly failed miserably, aside from the fact that yellow tokens are considered to be the strongest out of all due to their diversity and would probably win in any other fair/balanced bag builder. In fact, a fast engine (many red knight tokens for drawing efficiency or as stated above fast brown for gearwheels) is much stronger and also less dependant on the sandglass happenings. Fast priest loses heavily on a Plague and/or Sacristy draw on turns 2-4, which directly leads to my next point.

4. The sandglass tiles. I know many people like tight games. The feeling of being thrown back and forth, slapped in the face and having to do certain things at a certain time at all costs just to stay in the running for first place. But I am not that kind of person. I like games where you can find your own stategies, where everything can lead to victory and every route is fairly balanced. Or where obstacles are very minor and more often than not "easy" to overcome. E.g. like in Caverna (food) or ZhanGuo (numbers). I also do enjoy games that are a little tighter from time to time, like with the food in Le Havre or the black crystals in AquaSphere, but if it get's out of hand and "too frequent", it irritates and upsets me a little to be honest. Orleans has these moments of pure annoyance, when there are two plagues or three taxes right at the start of the game, which simply bothers me. The drawing of the tiles is pure luck and the randomness of this fact (also, again: in a heavy strategy game) just boggles my mind. Why not fix them with printed numbers and a pre-selection process before the game. 1 Plague in the first third of the game, one in the second, one in the last - without knowing, when exactly. This would be equally fine, but a much smoother and more satisfying game experience. The game in it's current state is too much of a luckfest here and there so that certain matches can be real downers, believe me. And I am not a person that would ever leave a game mid-match or that insults the game every round, never. But Orleans was the first game ever that had me on the edge of that, really. Thank god I don't have to play evil Feld's like In The Year Of The Dragon I guess..

5. Also, considering penalties, the "white track" feels highly unnecessary in my opinion. Firstly, it can be a real pain in the ass (sorry for my bad mouth here) in the first turns and secondly does it encourage players to fill their bag with useless fodder of whites, just to get some more goods tiles and escape the penalty of last place on this track. This becomes rather tiring and should really NOT be part of a bag builder, where engine(!) is your most important thing. Keeping your bag thin and knowing what tokens/cubes you have is essential to pave the way for you and to follow a consistent strategy. Three more whites on turns 10-13 can really slow you down considerably imho and this simply would not have been necessary, if you would have removed the penalty (plus reward for 1st seat here!) in the first place. Really annoying.

6. Peoples tokens that are left in your bag in the end of the game should be worth VP also. It is just sad that everything that is left in your bag is senseless after the "graveyard"-board is full, close to full or simply not accessible for you anymore. Hyperborea does a better job here imho, giving you an incentive to collect tokens (here: cubes). I know you could argue for Orleans here that it could be part of your engine to keep your bag small and therefore fast, absolutely, but in the end this point is simply personal preference I guess.

7. Also, considering the bag: being able to look into your bag or put stuff out to recount what you currently have left and will be able to draw in the next turn is a childrens decision. Keeping track of your cubes/tokens yourself is an essential part of bag building entirely and a huge portion of fun for me personally in special. Here you can truly see who calculates from turn to turn and which player keeps track of his cubes/tokens and which doesn't. A simple houserule could fix this also, but rules or variants should be in the official rulebook in the first place and not be tasks for the players afterwards, at least in my opinion. This is not to say that the game is weakly written or that the rules are bad, by far not, but in my personal opinion a true bag builder should keep his engine hidden by default. As simple as that.

8. A really small one for last, if we're getting nitpicky anyway: if every colour has it's own track, why not Yellow? I know, yellow tokens are considered to be the best/strongest etc., but having a yellow track would just be consistent from a game design standpoint and not having it ultimately feels a but lackluster or unsatisfying to be honest. I want to show everybody how many Priests I have, darn it..! ;) Please make it happen in the next expansion. It does not have to feature a bonus for each step anyway, just the visual representation would be nice (and fair!). Thanks.. ;)

To end on a positive note though, here are some things that I really enjoy in Orleans and that keep it from the lows of 5's or 6s', ratingwise... :

A: The map size. It is forgiving and fair for all players, even in a four player game. Although it's a little too big for high player interaction, it leaves enough breathing room to reship an enemy, especially if you heavily dislike this kind of direct gameplay. Personally, I would love more interaction - as stated in "1." - but the current map size is fine for me too, for abovementioned reasons.

B: Being able to leave pieces on the market if you don't want to place them yet.

C: Being able to leave pieces on your board if you don't want to take the action yet  (as for instance having to perform a mandatory reset like in Hyperborea and putting everything away, back in your bag).

D: Being able to instantly put new tokens plus the used ones back in your bag after taking an action - this guarantees a more smooth flow of resources (tokens/cubes) than in most bag builders I know.

E: Being able to increase the size of your drawn tokens/cubes per turn. I really, really(!) like this feature (and to name it: Red Knights!!).

F: The main end game scoring with "(houses+dudes) x golden markers" is really elegant. I enjoy it quite a bit.

G: Killing people off to thin your bag and also get some gold/books/dudes on the way is great and a much better version of this mechanism than for instance in Village imho.

H: There is little to no downtime because of fairly fast gameplay and always interesting decisions. The last four to five rounds can be a bit tiring, sure, but I don't see this as a real issue though. It's fun and quick enough, and there are no real time-killing brain burner turns of sorts like e.g. in Five Tribes sometimes.

I will come back to this comment if I want to add anything later. Consider me to update this in the next 1-3 days haha.
Thanks for reading in any event. Have a nice day.

RATING: 7
A bag building game where you start with some tokens and get more tokens that enable you to take more actions.  You add to a board to execute actions on the board and there is a scoring location in the center to eliminate old tokens that don't help you.  The game is a very interesting take on the "deck builder".  

I was not a big fan of the art, but I could see where others might like the medieval feel of it.  And the tokens did show a lot of wear after only a few plays.

RATING: 8
German di 120 minuti stile bag building.
Tutti i giocatori iniziano con un set di 4 gettoni di colori diversi e nel proprio turno, dopo aver estrato un certo numero di gettoni a caso dal proprio sacchetto, possono attivare una o più caselle azione utilizzando i gettoni dei colori richiesti. Tra le azioni disponibili si possono aggiungere altri gettoni al sacchetto, estrarne un numero maggiore, costruire edifici che sbloccano bonus o azioni personali per tutta la partita ed altro.
L'interazione con gli altri giocatori è prevista in piccola parte mediante un tracciato parallelo sul quale con le apposite azioni ci si può muovere, costruire avamposti e raccogliere risorse (chi primo arriva meglio alloggia)

RATING: 7
[b][thing=164928][/thing][/b] is one of 2014's crop of bag-builder games.  At it's heart, it's an worker placement game that uses chits and a bag in the same way a deck-builder uses a deck.  As workers are drawn at random from the player's bag, it's in the best interest of the player to hire workers that support his/her strategy.  And there's also a mechanism to retire workers to thin one's deck.  All in all, an interesting game that seems to have multiple strategies to explore.

RATING: 9
Don't know how I missed this one for as long as I did.  What an amazing game of pool building/worker placement!  The variety of building tiles adds a nice element of change between each game, but that is just icing on top of the cake.

Orleans gives players a wide array of objectives to shoot for and asks them to most efficiently, and craftily, complete them before the competition.

Unless some deckbuilders, it is easy in Orleans to thin out your "deck" in a way that makes your actions easier to predict, but also rewards you in a race with other players for more objectives.

Everything just clicks so well in Orleans that if you have not tried it, you honestly should.

RATING: 7
Rating based on 4 players.

A very pleasant game to play. It's a little low on player interaction for me, but the bingo-like matching of workers to perform actions is quite fun as you build your engine (though it's not quite an engine, as you never know which workers you'll draw), and while there's lots of things you can do it was not a point salad. I wish the chit-pull mechanism had more interesting things to do with the results... if the entire game was more like the map side with players jockeying for position I think I would like this game more. I think the game lasts a tad too long, but it was enjoyable.

Traded as I realize I don't really have opportunity to play this (as my own copy), and when I played it a second time it pretty much went down the same path as the first game.

RATING: 8
Straighforward engine builder.

I found the first half of the game fantastic in that you you have a huge, interesting array of different strategies. I suspect you could play this game a hundred times, you could come up with 100 different ways of building your engine.

If the game has a flaw, it's that the second half can become a bit samey. Said another way, by this point you are simply applying your engine turn after turn rather than making any strategic decisions. While the beneficial deeds board does give some interest in the second half, it's all tactical, point salad decisions at this point, which while not terrible, isn't at the sublime level of the game's beginning.

RATING: 6.8
This game has such potential.  On my first play, I would've rated it an 8.  With further plays, however, I've decided that the game isn't as polished as it could be.  Buildings and worker groups are imbalanced (even ignoring the entire Bath House history), and the game has scaling issues IMO.  There isn't as much variety as one would think since certain buildings are simply stronger than others.  There are incentives for things that don't need incentives, and a lack of incentives for strategies that could use them.  Additionally, the Events are practically meaningless except in the early game where they can feel devastating.  Money and goods are really just straight points without any interesting conversions, etc., and the map could be more interesting than it is IMO.  

Despite all of that, I'm keeping the game.  I like it... I just wish they had polished it up some more.  I'm curious to see if the new expansion will iron out some of the wrinkles...

RATING: 6
I have some problems with Orleans:

1. The random events are a nuisance and add nothing interesting to the game. My two problems with them are:

- Some bad luck with an early harvest event could have you losing $4 more than other players (if you haven't yet obtained a wheat tile), and an early plague could have you losing a good chit while others get lucky and lose nothing.

- They steer you away from a travel-based strategy as they cause you to lose money from having lots of goods tiles and gain money for being high on the development track and having lots of trading posts.

2. There should be more tension in competing for land/water travel routes. As is, there is no sense of pressure despite the seemingly intriguing random setup. There are just so many ways to score points that squeezing an extra point here or there from traveling in better directions isn't critical. It's actually more important to just not get pinned into a corner so that you can get your trading posts into play.

(Follow-up note - I've softened on this a bit, as I see that sometimes one path has much more valuable goods than another. Alternatively, you can use a technology wheel on either land or water and just use those paths for easier movement.)

3. You are most likely going to have to get to 4 or 5 on the development track and have a lot of trading posts (plus "person" chits) to win, both because of the end-game multiplier scoring and because of the way events reward players during the game. This, along with the availability of all level I/II black add-on tiles and all events always being the same makes each game feel similar.

I'm no game designer, but I envision a more dynamic game in which players start with more chits in the bag and have to decide whether to "donate" (trash) early to get a tight engine going or build up a big pool of chits. The optimal engine would depend strongly on the map setup, varied events for that particular game (which are drawn and known before the game begins), a limited number of level I/II tiles available, etc. I guess I'm looking for something more analogous to Dominion.

Despite my problems with the game, I give it a 6 for its attempts at innovation with the bag building mechanic.

RATING: 3
Orleans is a dull and boring point salad cube-pusher with a dubious theme.  The  "bag building" mechanism is merely a variation of deck building, which I hate. Yet it was clear 30 minutes into play I just wanted it to be over. BGG says it takes 90 minutes. Ha! My displeasure only grew as our four-player game went on for 2 hours and 35 minutes.

RATING: 7.5
Neat game where you pull tokens out of a bag, use them on your personal board to trigger powers (such as purchasing other tokens - like a deck-builder, travel across the map collecting goods, build trading posts, build buildings (allowing you actions that are only available to you, or send them on deeds (where they leave your pool but you gain status or money). It's interesting trying to optimize your token pool and how to tactically play the tokens you do draw. My big fear, and we'll need to see, is that it seems like this may be a game with an optimal strategy, almost like something you could program in ahead of time.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is a wholly abstract point-salad game.  You get get action-selection chits, put them in a bag and then randomly pull them.  The actions available to you are limited by what chits you pull.  Buying chits and putting them in your bag is supposed to be like "deck-building", but it is an inferior mechanism for 2 reasons: (1) you don't get enough or pull enough to make the differences in distribution significant. (2) you are not guaranteed to see a chit ever again.  In short, as a base mechanic, it is a bit too random for my taste.  That said, the game is a fine, unoffensive, solitary Euro with some in-game tension that comes from the frustrating chit-pulls.   
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
Orleans hatte mich durch die etwas biedere Aufmachung eher abgeschreckt. Ja, natürlich versuchen die Grafiken hier die mittelalterliche Kunst der Merowinger bzw Karolinger zu adaptieren und trotzdem, in Kombination mit dem eher cartoonhaften Stil, hat mich das Gesamtergebnis nicht angesprochen. Durch eine Rabattaktion, konnte ich Orleans dann sehr günstig erwerben und wollte dem Spiel eine Chance geben. Eine absolut richtige Entscheidung, denn Orleans ist ein großartiges Spiel. Die Mechanik mit Mix aus Worker-Placement und "Bag Building" - ja man sammelt unterschiedliche Arbeiterfiguren in seinem Stoffsäckchen" - ist toll und funktioniert einwandfrei. Die Spieldauer ist mit 16 Runden und ca 60 Minuten angenehm kurz, so dass man an einem Abend auch mehrere Partien hintereinander spielen kann. Die Aktionsmöglichkeiten erinnern ein wenig an Village, es gibt unterschiedliche Stationen, die man mit seinen Arbeitern aktivieren kann. So bietet Orleans eine Vielzahl an unterschiedlichen Strategien zum Sieg. Möchte man möglichst viele Städte besuchen, dort Kontore errichten und dabei wertvolle Warenplättchen einsammeln? Konzentriert man sich eher auf die Entwicklungsleiste, um dort seinen Faktor für die Siegpunkte zu erhöhen? Beteiligt man sich fokussiert an der Fertigstellung der Segensreichen Werke? Orleans ist Abwechslung pur, es ist Strategisch, doch nicht zu kompliziert. Ich empfehle auch unbedingt die Erweiterung, die spannende Szenarien für Solospieler, 2 Spieler oder Kooperative Spieler hinzufügt.

RATING: 8
In this bag building game, players generate people in medeival Orleans. The various types of people are monks, farmers, knights, scholars and so forth. The people go into a bag, and you only pull a certain amount of people out per turn to use. You use people to get more people, build trading posts, travel to collect goods or get more tiles to use them. 

This is a very tense game, with lots of choices and decisions. At some point you realize you are not getting the right people out of the bag or not enough of them and there are many things you have to pay attention to.
There are different strategies to pursue and it is very fun. Now, the events such as a plague could conspire against you to force you to lose a person you just recruited, or you can't get the right mix in your draws, but those sorts of games are rare, and generally you do have a chance to work around the bad luck. 

We do play with the advanced rule where everyone gets to remove a building which makes each game very interesting. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Setup took about 10 min and a little AP inducing. Gameplay was good though, but it felt a teeny tiny bit random with the draw. It was also hard to see the board's extra board where you sent people permanently since it took so much table space. It felt a bit sandboxy and replayable though. Deckbuilder esque and good engine game too.

Didnt play with all rules quite right as we put out all the tiles, even for 5p, so it wasnt as tight as expected.  There were some minor things we missed, but the mechanics are good and theme amd art interesting and cool!

After 3 plays, Im finding that even though its a bit sandboxy, there is a limit to the number of effective strategies. I did wool and cloth all game for 9 pts a turn or usually even more but still lost semi significantly to guildhalls. I just wonder how truly sandboxy and freeform it is. Downtime is still simpler and easier to deal with and engine aspect is cool still but I found Dominion's engine simpler, faster, more variety and probably more fun imo (though this doesnt drag like some Dominion games do)

Imo its a solid 7 ish, but not more.

EDIT: Reading some strategy related stuff, it seems development track is nigh necessary in some regard and that there may just be 1 or so other strategies aside from it (dev track gives most points with multiplier and lots of money from events). Events toward end are nigh worthless and things like plague and draw are a bit annoying. I'm gonna trade this now because of all these things. Also large table footprint and setup, and the goods aren't honestly differentiated - they're just points...

RATING: 7.25
this game currently has a rare (for a euro) 8.0 or higher average rating, and I'm not quite seeing it, or it shows I'm a step off from the mainstream preference here with the way I favor tactical gameplay. This game is almost a pure engine builder, with very little opportunity for game-generated variation (as opposed to the euros with highly randomized elements in the initial setup/gameplay, which encourage decisions at a tactical level in reaction to the randomization). The variation here must then come from the other players in the game, and I'm gathering that there is a yearning for games such as those, in which players play each other rather than each other and the game. Or perhaps I'm discounting the "populace-shaping" element (aka deckbuilding/bagbuilding), which I don't much favor (I like *other* elements of Eminent Domain and Trains, and dislike Hyperborea, Dominion, and CCGs universally) At any rate, my experience was: I liked the simultaneity of it but chafe at the scripted nature of the engines. It reminds me of Hansa Teutonica, which I rated similarly. That said, my rating isn't far off from the average, but usually with modern euros like these, I find I'm rating with a 1-2 point premium to the average.

RATING: 7
This one has potential but - at least in a two player game - it seems to have issues.   The map is not very tight so its very easy to build trading posts in any city.  If you can acquire some monks, then it seems very easy to take any action you want.   And with so many rounds, the last several all seem to end up being the user taking the same actions to generate points.    There's a lot to like conceptually with this one, but it doesn't seem to work well for us at two players.

We last played by restricting the map to the top of the board, and removing three random time tiles for a shorter, tighter game.  This helped some, but the trading posts was still the winning strategy.   We're really not sure about this one.

RATING: 8.2
There are complaints about the quality of the components, but I found them to be quite good in my French version.

Some people say it's a polished version of [thing=119788]Hyperborea[/thing], but they are only similar in their bag-building mechanism, and even that works in a different way. Orléans is an original game with a perfect mix of strategy, tactic, interaction and luck. It's one of those games where you want to do all actions, you're already thinking 2-3 turns ahead and you can't wait until you do this and that. But watch out! If your opponents do this other thing before you, you would lose this benefit to them!

Games are quite fluid, even with 4 players, because most of the turn happens simultaneously and the number of players will not affect its lenght as much as in other games. Nevertheless, there are some slower moments while some player thinks over a very difficult decision.

Orléans promises many different paths to victory, but I'm not sure about that. I don't think there's a winning strategy, but they are not infinite either. In any case, all your actions will be influenced by the random pieces you draw each turn, the random events and the quite unpredictable actions of your opponents; rest assured: there will be not 2 games alike.

Overall, I'm quite hapy with it up to now and I'm looking forward to play it more and explore different strategies.

First impression [url=http://boardgamegeek.com/geeklist/184429/item/4274914#item4274914]here[/url].

RATING: 8
[b]Orleans[/b]

[COLOR=#FF0000]A year later... thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]I'm up in the air with Orleans and would like to get an opportunity to play again so that I can decide what I think (this hasn't happened in part because I don't own the game and my buddy sold his copy). I definitely enjoyed both plays, but wonder if certain paths lead to victory while others seem to be much less viable. As far as bag builders go this is by far my favorite, I really enjoy how the different mechanisms of the game are quite intuitive. Orleans isn't a game that needs repeated play before you become proficient making it a great light to medium weight Euro.

I came close to buying this after playing, but I have so many games of this weight that I'm not sure it would get played all that often and I know that I could probably get some folks to play it in my game group. I am enamored with the bag building mechanism, but I think I'll continue to pass for now until I have a chance to play a few more times.[/i]

[COLOR=#FF0000]Initial thoughts and rating (8):[/COLOR]

[i]A highly enjoyable bag building game. The use of tokens and a bag removes the tedium of constant card shuffling and offers a nice tactile element to the game. There seem to be multiple strategies to explore, but I have one concern in regards to this. Since all the buildings are available from the beginning of each game (similar to Caverna) I wonder if certain buildings will become "must buys" because they are more powerful/versatile than others. I've only played once so I am not sure, but looking through the buildings there definitely seem to be some that appeared weaker than the others. Time will tell, and again I am not even sure it is an issue. I do know that I'm definitely looking forward to more plays.

Quick update - I actually got a second play in right at the end of the month, this time with 3 players. This play alleviated some of my concerns with some buildings being much better than others. We all tried different strategies and buildings and in the end I think we were only separated by 16 points. Pretty impressive for a game with a lot of different scoring opportunities. After this play I am closer to picking up a copy because the rule set is very intuitive, making it easy to teach/learn and after two plays there seems to be a lot of different strategies you can explore.[/i]

RATING: 8.5
規則:
*起始放人物跟科技板塊在對應位置，事件卡17張洗勻，朝聖放一張在最上面，物品板塊隨機放在陸路跟水路上(對應人數)，剩下的分類擺在對應位置，建築物分類放好，13市民板塊放在對應位置
*3人 移除3個僧侶騎士學者，其他人物移除2個，隨機移除6個物品
*2人 移除6個僧侶騎士學者，其他人物移除4個，隨機移除12個物品
*每個玩家拿對應顏色板塊跟token跟一布袋，5塊錢，人物都放在市集，商人起始在奧爾良，科技跟人物軌都在第一格
*遊戲進行18輪，每輪7個階段，年紀輕的起始
1 起始玩家開一張事件卡
2 人口普查 農民軌第一加一元，最低付一元，平手都不用，兩人不用扣錢
3 跟隨者 根據騎士軌從袋中抽對應數目跟隨者放在布袋，跟隨者不能超過市集容量
4 規劃 把市集的人物放在自己圖版或建築物對應位置，可不放留在市集
5 行動 起始玩家開始，輪流做已啟動的行動，做完跟隨者丟回袋中，pass就不能再做，沒做的可留到之後輪做
*獲得人物板塊，移動對應人物軌，獲得上面的物品
*僧侶可取代任何人物，但是沒有對應人物軌可移動
*工匠軌獲得的科技板塊第一個要取代農民軌，第二個之後可取代僧侶一外的格子，限制是一定要是兩個人物以上啟動的行動，且只能取代一格，且不能移動
*學者會提升研發軌，過錢拿錢，過分為最後計分，或事件影響
*移動都是移動一格，並拿取路上的一個物品
*除了奧爾良(大家都能有一個)，每個城鎮只能有一個交易站
*市鎮大廳跟火藥塔(可當作市集多容納兩個)可送人到名人堂，不用去同一個，去之後拿對應資源，完成最後一個得拿市民板塊，這裡僧侶不能取代其他人物板塊，不能用起始人物來放
6 事件 結算事件
*瘟疫事件如果沒得抽或抽到起始人物都沒事
*沒錢付時 可付交易站(沒蓋也可)人物板塊(袋中抽起始不行)，物品，建築物，科技板塊，移除遊戲
7 起始玩家標記左傳
*遊戲最後分數 物品 布5-羊毛線4-酒3-起士2-麥1，1元1分，市民+交易站，每個分數等於發展軌上星號分
*只要物品或人物不夠，該動作就不能做
*可隨時看袋中狀況
*可選擇少抽人，每少抽一個可移動一個人回市集
*額外規則，起始可以輪流banned掉一個建築物，兩人一個banned兩個
*官方變體規則，事件卡分成六種一疊洗勻(第一張一樣)，除朝聖跟瘟疫外事件結算跟發生次數有關，
收稅 一個貨物付一元，兩個付一元，三個付一元
收成 付一個食物(5元)，付兩個食物(10元)，付三個食物(15元)
交易 一個交易站三元，一個交易站兩元，一個交易站一元
歲收 收入*3，收入*2，收入一樣
*抽三選一建築改動 (變成二取一 或是 移到時代二)
*規劃階段如有需求可以按順位做

RATING: 8.5
Bag-building, yay! In Orleans, every player tries to make as many actions you can every round. Every action takes at least two of your pieces, either farmers, workers, scholars, or monks. The actions are either getting more pieces (kinda like your workforce), upgrading the power of your pieces, getting spaces or "buildings" only for you. For any Euro fan, I think Orleans is a must. Almost every aspect of the game reminded me of my favorite Euros: the traveling mechanic from Concordia, deck-building from Dominion, influence tracks from Terra Mystica. Although the randomness of your draws may impact your strategy for your turn, I found it as a challenge; what steps can I take to lower the randomness for my draws and use the pieces efficiently? I feel like most Euros reward the 
"A Little Bit Of Everything" strategy, so I'm glad to see that Orleans run the opposite way. It's better to triumph in one dimension of the game, then do okay in all of them. I love Orleans because it satisfied my taste for a Euro, but it bent the game in little, sometimes frustrating ways. Which I loved. Orleans is a great addition to the Euro genre and I definitely recommend it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I liked it a lot, but am hesitant about rating it higher in the fear that it's too sandbox-y to provide a substantially different playing experience from game to game. With all tech immediately available, there are many paths to explore to determine which approach of tech or non-tech paths provides best efficiency. But the only game randomizer is the order in which the events come out which you have no control over and may either hurt or hinder your strategy unknowingly. There's are interactive race elements with board presence and finishing engine tracks, but these are slow-to-develop races. Maybe that's enough, don't know. But it at least provides multiple paths to explore in the first instance and that's half the battle won. My fear is that the second half of riding that learning curve may be a little too abstract to engender replay. Happy to play, but I don't need to buy.

RATING: 4
Thought this would be a lot heavier than it turned out to be. Set up is a bitch, by far the longest of any of my games and that includes Trajan. The low complexity is not worth the long set up time. 

Pluses:

- great components. Why are people complaining about components? Thick tiles, nice bags. I Actually went "wow!" when opening the box. Punching tiles was very easy, no rips, and there is smooth satin finish to everything that makes handling them a pleasant tactile experience.  
- bag builder mechanic is a first for me and fun.
- lof ear strategy when you have few workers. 
- game teaches fast and plays very fast with two. Definitely a gateway game.

Neg

- very lame events. Only one, the plague, is hitting hard and only if it happens early. We didn't hurt or care about any of the others. Never had a torture. Money is abundant.
- very solitaire. The only time I watch opponent is over the buildings (townhall) placement, as first player moving can complete a building first and take the citizen borfre you get it,
- everything is worth simple points. It's just a race to get the best goods.
- I fell for the hype on this and don't think it deserves it. Close to '100 ranking'on bgg is far too high, and an obvious 'cult of the new' ranking. It's a simple, gateway game and I don't really like gateways. Not much thinking involved, though that might be a plus for some.
- Some buildings are very weak, like sacristy. There is no reason to build this with events not hurting you. 
- easy to forget to pull back workers from your extra building action spaces as the tiles sit near your main board and are not as easily spotted. I left two monks on a tile for three turns before I noticed they were still there. 
- buying many trading houses is clearly a winning strategy. This cannot be ignored.
- map route competiton nonexistant with 2 players. Too easy to build a path and head off on your own. 
- once you have many workers, choices are all obvious and fairly easy to do.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
Played once so far, three players: 
I like the mechanics buying different action-chits and drawing them from a bag. It's exactly like Hyperborea cube drawing. 
Unfortunately it becomes a little repetitive and the game feels too long. 

Have played it a few more times now and it grows on you so I have to raise my rating a little. 

The place tiles are quite unbalanced, which might even kingmake the starting player. With the "advanced" rules that each player may discard one place tile before the game starts solves that somewhat.  

Have played this quite some times now, and even though it was one of the EuropeMasters games for 2015 (wich usually mean you're fed up with the game after all practising) I still enjoy it, and have changed my rating accordingly. 

All miniexpansions changes the game a lot. Maybe one should add a random set of place tiles each game to make it more varied. The extra money in the new edition and the expansion changes the game a lot too, since more than one person can use a money gathering strategy without running out. My friend experienced this in a bad way at EuropeMasters, since we practised with the first edition, but competed with the new edition, which made it possible for two persons to gather over 70 coins. Both beat my friend.

RATING: 8
4 Plays - a bag building/ engine building game with a variety of strategies to try. The events, variety of buildings and variable map setup will allow for quite some replayability, though not endless. For players who play many times, the replayability will be in the strength of the gameplay and group dynamics (which can affect game play choices). 

My only nitpick, particularly for new players, are the numerous buildings each with a unique ability. These didn't seem accessible to me on my first few plays, as I just didn't care to look through so many different options when I could just ignore them and do something else. Having so many tiles laid out beside the board also just makes a game appear messy, fiddly and more visible to some players than others. This is something I really don't like about Caverna. Perhaps keeping the building tiles in two stacks on the board, along with each player having an aid of some kind could solve this.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Orleans is a "pouch-building" game--throughout the game you acquire tokens of different types to go into your pouch, and each turn you pull a certain number of tokens out of your pouch which determine what kind of actions you can do.  Players have a player board where they assign their tokens to various actions, all of which require multiple different tokens to fire.  Most of the action spaces allow a player to take a new token of a specific type, and advance on that token's track, each of which have different abilities.  Some of the track abilities include taking more tokens out of the bag each turn, being able to choose a building tile that gives you another possible action, end-game multiplier bonuses, etc.  There were a few action spaces that didn't give you more tokens--these let you move your pawn around a map board to pick up goods chits (worth points) or establish trading posts (for the end-game multiplier), and a final action spot let you "retire" tokens to a central board for a couple of points (this seemed more aimed at giving players something to do on the last turn, rather than allowing a hardcore pouch-thinning strategy).  Overall, I really enjoyed this game--it's not really too ambitious, but it executes the pouch-building idea really well, and it was fun to build up on the tracks and gain new action spaces.  I worry about replay value though, as some tracks and buildings seemed a lot better than others.

(4 plays)

RATING: 7
Interesting take on the "deck building" genre. Getting various pieces that you draw out of a bag and immediately put back in once you use them really makes you consider how you're building your bag. You may continue to draw the old tiles that you just used instead of the new ones that you bought. I liked how everything was open, although it did feel a bit solitaire.

You know, for a game that isn't really THAT unique, I appreciate the open-endedness of it and I look forward to trying out some unique/interesting combos or strategies. I worry there might be a "correct" way to play, but I have yet to figure it out yet.

Includes the deluxe wooden pieces and 5th player kit.

RATING: 8.5
Slight downgrade, the freshness wears of a bit after 10 games or so.

***

It's fun to control and manage the followers in your bag. Trick is to think ahead and maximize the chances to draw the ones you want when you need them. Sometime you need to send some of the less desirable ones of to beneficial deeds or make sure some of them are not returning to the bag by leaving them on spots/track on the player board, maybe restrain to activate a "ready" building for a round, just so they don't "pollute" the bag. Keeping track of the turnorder and who is doing what is important. The pulverturm (gunpowdertower) is a powerful building.. 

Two 2-player and two 3-player games so far.. it is getting better with each play. 

Love the art
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Very interesting concept of bag-building -> chit-pulling -> chit combinaton allocation to available actions. This game does not have the common ground with other deck/pool building games in the sense that you dont actually go through all the chits in your bag. Most of the chits go back to your bag each turn (there is no discard pile). So for example, if you have acquired 5 monks (a powerful, wildcard chit) and have a total of 15 chits, and draw 5 per turn you could still possibly pull only 1 of them during three turns. So this adds some luck element to the game. Then again the many different possibilities to allocate chits and score points mitigate this luck somewhat and make it a solid and strategical game.

RATING: 9
Call me a cube fetischist, but pushing cubes and building bags full of useful followers is truly exiting! Orléans is streamlined and easy to grasp but makes for a challenging set of decisions. Maybe the strongest point of the game is the sense of accomplishment you get not only at the end of the game, but also during play. A game I feel has great longevity and I always look forward to the next session. One point from a perfect 10 because of lengthy set-up time and the added edit below.

Edit: Only negative, as with any strategy game with minimal luck (or at least lots of ways to mitigate luck with draws from the bag), is that you can easily fall into the same, or very similar, strategy for every game. So it helps if players are at the same level. This can also be mitigated by taking out 1-2 buildings/player each game, possibly limiting strategy somewhat.

RATING: N/A
P: 2-4, T: 90 min, KenSdJ-N
Engine building, worker placement, 'bag/pool-building', medium heavy Euro. Similar to deck building, but here your recruited workers go into a grab bag (fairly unique). Resource Scarcity! Various strategies and options to explore. Low player interaction, 2 hour optimization puzzle. Multi track/mechanic boards (map - route building, 'good deeds' - a la cult track Terra Mystica, progress tracks)

-Theme irrelevant, long set up time, no variable set up (same puzzle each time). Lacking English components (original edition, check carefully). Fiddly end game scoring. Can be AP prone, turns get longer as game progresses (game can slow down and may overstay its welcome), player skill/familiarity will produce some lopsided scores.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This game was really fantastic. You spend these worker tokens on your player board to activate actions, then you put your tokens back into your bag to be redrawn randomly in future turns. You have a limited space for workers "on deck" so to speak, so you need to keep your guys busy, otherwise you wind up with wasted draws. You also get to construct new action spaces for yourself, and you get to put little wheels on locations, which act as a permanent worker for that spot alone, helping you ease the need for excessive worker type diversity. The real goal of the game is to get houses, or man tokens, and then amp your multiplier up, before the event clock runs out. The game plays very very fast, so although at the start of the game the event pile looks high, before you know it, it's empty. The upgraded tokens from the KS will be a very welcome change to the game, as the small worker tokens could sort of get stuck in the corner of the bag if you didn't jangle it around severely enough. Currently I feel like I'd be willing to play this again pretty much anytime.

:star: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:star: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::star::nostar: Enjoyment

RATING: 6
An interesting bag building / engine building game. I like the variety of options, and the way that they build on each other to give a real sense of progress over the course of the game. I don't like the fairly low interaction between players, and how much time it takes to look through all the options. Especially for new players, you have to either decide not to explore a lot of the options or just take a very long time on your turn reading through them and asking what the more complicated ones do. It's definitely the kind of game you need to play a couple of times before you understand most of the potential combos. It also takes quite a while to set up and tear down, and the end game scoring feels a bit anticlimactic and unfulfilling for how long the game takes to play.

RATING: 8
Uses tokens and a bag rather than a deck builder. Most actions require multiple tokens to fire. What you can do is limited by the tokens drawn from the bag. 
Not very interactive since each player has his own board and actions. The primary interaction is a race to get bonuses first and when tokens begin to run out. Before limits kick in you don't have pay too much attention to turn order.
However, it does fit together well, you can win in all the tracks like most worker placement you still need to work counter cyclic to other players. The limited draw means you need to use the efficiently as possible and perhaps save some for a bigger next turn.
The different tracks allow you to get more tokens some are only available that way. Get a building to get additional actions, end game multipliers, move a pawn around to build houses for scoring and collect goods for scoring. Retire tokens to bonus areas, bunches of ways to score points

Streamlined and enjoyable to play, but glad to play someone else's copy and have no need to own

RATING: 5
Unfortunately this game is not for me. 
Its bland, uninteresting, too loose to offer any meaningful decisions. 
Somehow I don't find it interesting to pull out chits from a bag, just to place them somewhere, so that I can buy more chits into my bag. 
The culling mechanics is there, but again, no tension there, you are putting the chits on a separate board in a loose race for filling up rows with pre-defined chits for each position. 
Then there is also a board with a map of the county around Orleans where you can race for grabbing goods and laying down contorhouses, but again, pretty bland.... :(
 This is the kind of point salad which usully leave me utterly cold: a billion ways to get points but no central, overarching  mechanics which elevates this game from being mediocre. 
This also means, that Alteplano is also out of my wishlist: the game is from the same designer, the fundation of both games is the same (bag-building), and according to the general opinion Alteplano seems to be the worse game from the two....

Great game! For a short while I though it had replaced Roll for the Galaxy, but I eventually came around deciding I like RftG better just because of the sheer variability in RftG.

I love Orleans, I really do - but I don't think it's a game I want to play over and over as the variability ain't as great at it might appear. I definitely recommend always removing a few buildings before the game begins to force different tactics.

I disagree with the complaints about luck of the draw - it is very easy to trim your bag to fit just the strategy you're going for. Sure, you'll have that odd round where you just don't draw those monks you really need, but with 18 rounds it's not an issue.

RATING: 6
I'll rate this a 6 after one play. I have a horrible feeling that 6 is a very mean score to give. But I just can't get beyond the fiddliness of aspects of Orleans.

Orleans is a nice, sedate game of worker placement, area control and engine-building ... but one that has a messy 'interface'.

At the start of each round players draw many of their tokens out of their bag randomly and then place them in colour combinations on their tableu/buildings in order to activate various abilities/cubes. It is time-consuming and hard to plan for. And is it OK to peek at where other players are putting theirs, as it may be important? You then resolve those actions on various shared boards/tracks.

If that makes it sound like a chore, it kind-of is. Which is a shame, as there are plenty of options and combinations with which to experiment.

You can get a similar, less fiddly game experience from something like New Bedford (watch out for its huge errata) or Lords of Waterdeep. Or, better, how about the excellent Caylus or Bruxelles 1893.

RATING: 6.5
Very well designed Euro game with bag-building as the central mechanism. All mechanism work really well and it is certainly a good game, but to me in Orleans all mechanisms have no thematic integration at all and the game is a soulless victory point salad: There is some point track that in the multiplies with the number of buildings you have for VPs at the end; there are different councils to send your workers too; if you complete a council you get a person that also multiplies with the track; goods give you points; money give you points; there are special building for every single mechanism of the game; etc. In the end, Orleans combines all negative aspects a (still well working) Euro can have.

Update: Have played it again and I liked it a bit more. But still there is little interaction, which is restricted to racing on different tracks. The action mechanism is kind of innovative, but to me not really exciting. It feels similarly disconnected as all leading mechanisms in Feld games do.

RATING: 7.8
(+) Bag builder with some really nice and interesting engine building aspects

(+) Different ways to approach the game

(+) Accessible: structured game flow, easy rules, reasonable play time, yet challenging enough

(-) Some luck dependence due to some of the events.

(-) A little too much luck dependence due to the fact that your bag doesn't run out before you refill. This means the same workers can come out over and over again, while others remain 'stuck' in the bag. In my opinion this game would have been a lot better if used workers go in a seperate 'used workers pool' per player, and only get recycled when the entire bag runs out.

(-) If you want to score points with the book-track and houses: make sure you go for houses first! Bear in mind that the university track in order to move up on the book-track can also run out, but this will only be a problem in the second half of the game (and can be mitigated with certain tiles).

Approx game time: About 30 min / player

RATING: 7
A fun bag-builder, but I found playing the game was a bit more samey than I was expecting when learning the game.  Once you get your drawing # up, and maybe building a few buildings, you kind of max out your engine building capacity and are just trying to get as many guild halls down and max your tech level before the game ends.  Getting the people worth points is an interesting competition, but it also breaks the game in that you're supposed to be able to allocate your discs to actions simultaneously but because of this competition you need to wait to see if other players are going to beat you to the limited town hall spaces.  I feel like the game would have better if we were mostly competing for common places, like a standard worker placement game (although that would require some way to identify which people are yours to reclaim), but the general modern trend is for more multiplayer solitaire, optimizing your own boards, hence its popularity.

RATING: 8
Orleans is a fairly abstract game where players draw different types of workers from a bag and place them on their own player boards to  different actions from traveling the countryside to educating there population training their villagers and  improving their clan.  The game  plays up to 4 players where they try to expand their population across the board at the same time trying increase the value of each settlement or nobleman.

Pros: Fun action selection mechanism with drawing workers from the bag, Creative point value balancing mechanism, Deep decision making, Sandbox style gameplay allows players to play however they would like.

Cons: long set up time.  Lengthy Rules Explanation

RATING: 7
Not bad, but not very exciting either. It's a solid points salad/worker salad game that gamers who enjoy low interaction planning games might really like. The more players the more interaction, so that's a good thing. The dice and cards have been replaced by a bag pulling mechanism. There's multiple paths to securing victory points, but first you're going to need to acquire a few more workers because it's 18 rounds long and you'll need to assemble a workforce before you start worrying about the points too much. There's some timing elements, especially in the Rathaus, and that's a good thing too. It's okay.

For me the game is saved by the technology tiles (craftsmen), and the place tiles. The race to grab the choice place tiles in particular lifts the game from drab to somewhat exciting. Everything is a race, but at the same time it all feels quite leisurely. Like Castle sit Burgundy this game has short quick turns that keeps the pace up and stops players from catching up on sleep. (I don't think I could stand it with the five player expansion.) There's always something you can do, something you probably should do and probably a way you can do it. 

Decent game that holds up to Mombasa and Castles of Burgundy. But it might be my last bag pulling game because it does the job well and one is enough. Scores tend to be close as players weave their different paths through the points maze. I'm a fan of the simple, logical rules and ease of jumping back into a game after a long break when the rules might be a bit foggy. The family seem to enjoy it more than I do.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 1.5
Q: What do you get if you cross a cube pushing engine building euro with a Dominion - an engine building deck builder? 
A: A box chock full of cardboard chits and wooden pieces and stuff. 

Orleans a.k.a. Dominion the boardgame, reminds you that cards were there for a reason (making the game cheaper and smaller). Basically you're buying people tokens, throwing them in a bag and pulling them out, and you need 2-3 of a specific kind to make an action. From there on it's pretty much a type of restricted Dominion - in the first part you're building an engine, later you're trying to get points; restriction of possible options allows for a few different takes on building the whole bag drawing thingy. There's no interaction and after an adding points exercise somebody wins.  

So, if you thought Dominion is too streamlined, elegant and too reminiscent of CCGs, here's it's done in an old school euro vibe with one of the drabbest visuals ever (worse than Concordia). There are progression spread sheet charts filling the board, there's a lot of stuff arranged in neat lines, waiting to be picked up, and somewhere in the air there hovers at least an impression of many choices - as is the trend in dreary euros these days. The game has one thing going for it - it is more strategic than all tactical VPS games coming out these days, even if shares certain piece galore symptoms with them.

On one hand it's refreshing too see a more strategic oriented euro, on the other hand it's got all the symptoms of boring old school euros. Yawn. I must say there's a serious lack of a frowny eurodude on the cover.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6.5
Played once with 4P. The central mechanism of Orléans is bag-building, where players pull tokens out of a bag every round and create specific combinations of tokens to take actions. Like all deckbuilders, there are ways to gain more tokens and eliminate them.

While I'm usually big on deckbuilders, I'm not quite sold on Orléans. The bagbuilding aspect is really just a means to the end of pursuing the different VP paths in the game, and I rarely prioritised the token I was taking in whenever I took an action.  The one bright spot worth mentioning is that the Beneficial Deeds board is a clever way to reward players for culling tokens.

Beyond the bagbuilding aspect is an ordinary paint-by-numbers Euro, where player generally race to go up on a variety of tracks. There is no doubt Orléans is a game which was simply waiting to be designed, but I'm not sure if the end result is worth the anticipation.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8
[4 plays] A chip rather than card based version of Dominion crossed with Goa, Village, La Citta, and Hansa Teutonica. I've really enjoyed learning this game.

I think there are some optimal moves for the first several turns, and some overall strategies that are fairly imperative, but beyond that, to win you will need to focus on doing things a little differently from what everyone else is doing. I enjoy the arc of the game also, like many other great games (St. Pete) where you need to eventually cross over from building your engine to a purely point scoring finish.

Most recent play Jan '17.

RATING: 6
[2015-06-14]
Játékostáramnak a Burgund, nekem a Vulgari ugrott be elsőnek a játékról. Mindkettő jogosan. A Vulgari a tábla miatt. Itt is kapunk egy térképet, amin tudunk mászkálni, itt terményeket gyűjtünk az utunk során és kereskedőházakat tudunk létrehozni a városokban. A tábla másik része különböző típusú lépegetősáv, amin adott számú lépést megtéve bónuszokat kapunk. Egyrészt általában egy embert, amiket gyűjtve és zsákba tárolva adott számút húzva körönként akciókat hajthatunk végre, másrészt plusz bónuszokat. A játék pakliépítős alapokon nyugszik, ami itt emberépítő, mert munkásaink zsákból húzásával tudunk akciózni a saját kis táblánkon, itt a Burgundos beütés. Egy-egy akcióhoz általában több emberre van szükségünk és lehet a táblánkat akár bővíteni is további akcióhelyekkel. Kapunk még egy kegysávot is, ami a házaink és segítőink számát szorozza meg a játék végén az elért helyünkkel. Illetve van egy piac rész is, ahova már megunt munkásainkat küldhetjük pénzért, tulajdonképpen ez a pakliépítés rész rossz lapok kidobása akciója. Minden körben van még egy esemény is, ami többször negatív mint pozitív. Mi már a szerző által megreformált variánssal játszottunk, ami ezen lapokat egy meghatározott sorrendbe rakja és némi szorzót is ad a játék időben haladása alapján. A játék működik, de sok újat nem mutat és kicsit én teszteletlennek éreztem. Egyrészt nekem a kegysáv erősnek tűnik, másrészt az akcióválasztó rész elvileg szimultán, pedig egyáltalán nem mindegy ki milyen akciókat választ és hogyan hajtja végre. Mintha a Shogunban is nyílt lenne az akcióválasztás rész. Bár végülis a játék során nem éreztünk ebből nagy problémát, azért mindenképp érdekes. Az egyes plusz akciókat adó lapok is eléggé kiegyensúlyozatlanok. Ettől függetlenül játszható a játék és nem utasítanék vissza vele egy következő partit, de nem érzem kiemelkedőnek.

[2017-02-03]
Jó sok idő után ismét előkerült. A szimultán akcióválasztást továbbra is hibának tartom. Muszáj lenne sorban haladni, mert nagyon nem mindegy, hogy az előttem levő milyen akciót vesz el előlem. Ezt egyértelműen a játék hosszának rövidítése miatt rakták be, de sajnos sokat számít. Ebben a játékban alig kerültek elő plusz táblahelyek így ezek kiegyenlítettsége kevésbé jött ki, de mivel választható melyiket vesszük el, így aki előbb rámegy annak előnye van, de ez így rendben is van. Másodikra kicsit jobban bejött, de még nem jött el a pontemelés. Talán majd ha sikerül teljesen hibátlanul játszani. :)

RATING: 7
1 play, 4er

Very cool pouch building game. Like Hyperborea, but seems a bit more polished (although the components aren't as good!).

Each player has a bag and some starting worker tiles. Each of the 18 rounds, draw a number of tiles. Everyone simultaneously allocates them on their personal boards, to various actions. In order, activate the actions which have all the required workers. These allow you to recruit additional workers (place in bag), acquire goods tiles, build buildings, increase your tile drawing power, etc. 

There isn't any direct interaction; rather, you're competing with each other for board position and set collection. But it all works very well!

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a bag-building pseudo-worker placement euro game.  Each round players take a number of disks depicting different types of workers from a bag and place them on different action spaces requiring different types of workers.  Most of these spaces add new kinds of workers to your bag.  This is very similar to the game Hyperborea but with a much more European theme.

The game is very good.  I really like the bag-building mechanic (I also quite like Hyperborea) and there are many different strategies to follow so gameplay offers a decent amount of variety.  It also maintains a decent helping of luck of the draw to keep the game from being too deterministic or solvable.  Plus it forces you to think in similar terms to any deck builder about the balance between more options and draw efficiency.  There is even a way to "trash" disks from your bag (and get money and potential points from them).

If I had one criticism about the game it would be that it lasts a few too many rounds.  That's not to say that it feels too long.  Many of the phases in this game can be played simultaneously so there is little downtime and even with 4 players it didn't feel too long.  But by the end of the game all the most sought after workers were bought up, most people had maxed out the book track, and ~80% of the deeds track was full.  I would have liked it a little better if it was a little tighter and a little more difficult to drain every last point out of the game by the end.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Kudos to the designer for pulling this new mechanism out. A chit pulling system/bag building that is something new for boardgamers. This is obviously, IMO, the biggest compliment I have for Orleans.

On the downside is the lack of strategy in the game. I had the feeling that this could have been much better than what actually felt it is, mainly because it lacked depth and meaning to each action. Getting money is not an economic necessity, it's a VP thing. Moving within the map is not to get different circumstances but to get VP's. Even trashing actions, which works pretty well, gets you VP's.
Everything means VP's and there's nothing really strategic about the actions players are performing. It's mainly tactical.

But in the end, I rather remember the greatness behind this novel mechanism. Great.

(N)

RATING: 5
"Bag builder" game where action discs are drawn from a player's bag o' stuff, thus ensuring that perfect planning is impossible and you will never know if the winner managed it by skill or luck. Orleans is pretty clearly an evolution of the designer's earlier Siberia, and the bag-building mechanism seems much less clunky here -- various options for placement grant new discs, extra discs, new places to put them and so on. A map and some tiles provide assorted ways to get VP, represented by goods (which can't be spent) and money (which can't be spent).

It's smooth as butter, but really nothing that we haven't seen before. Good if you want an engine-builder where you can't be sure you'll get the right fuel for it to run.

RATING: 6.5
Fun game with an interesting premise and fairly unique mechanism. My biggest problem is two fold. One, the game seems a bit scripted and lacks a lot of replayability (since all the buildings come out and there isn't much randomness that mixes it up). The randomness that is there with the events can be overly swingy -- such as early plagues giving one person a huge advantage over the others with other people having no real way to catch up or "attack" them to balance it. And second, it feels a bit too much like a point salad. I wish there was more to do with the money and other resources other than them just being worth a certain amount of points. Besides that I enjoy it especially considering how fast it is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Orleans has been hailed as the first "bag-building" game - a mix of Dominion's deck-building (applied to types of workers) and a somewhat finicky worker placement system. Players can send their workers to do various tasks like hire even more workers, gather money and resources, open buildings that provide new action spaces, implement technologies (which are not very different from gaining extra workers), accumulate culture points, and move on a map to build trading posts. It's a good system, there's a good balance between planning out what workers you need to hire, keep, and send away (and in what order), and the uncertainty introduced by the fact that all of these workers are pulled randomly from your bag. The worker placement phase is semi-simultaneous, so downtime is minimized nicely, and there's a lot of different ways to get points (although generally you will go through all of them). 

My main criticism of Orleans is that it attempts to present a wide variety of systems, but instead of tying them together in a coherent way, almost all the elements (except for the workers themselves) don't really do anything during the game. For example: You can get coins from various places. What can you do with your coins? Can you spend them to buy things, to open new buildings or to hire better workers? Well not really, they are just worth points in the end. Another example: There are five kinds of goods that you can pick up as you explore the map, or are given by certain actions. What can you do with the goods? Can you sell them, convert them, trade them? Well not really, they are just worth points at the end. And another example: There's a map where you and your opponents can race around, building trading posts along the way. What do the trading posts do? Do they produce resources, or are you trying to make a connected network? Well, not really, they are just worth points at the end. 

My second criticism is that Orleans doesn't really have enough variability to shake up the game between plays. I really enjoyed my first learning game. Then I enjoyed my second game, where (now understanding the game system), I mapped out the most efficient build order of workers, upgrades and buildings. Then in my third game... it was about the same. The early game puzzle doesn't change at all. The late game puzzle doesn't change unless an opponent grabs a building you wanted (but since buildings are plentiful, you can just get your next choice, no big deal). That's about the extent of your interaction with your opponent; there's nothing impactful to fight over, no precarious plans to concoct or disrupt, no undercutting, no broken dreams. It's all just a steady accumulation of points, and it feels the same every time.

I'd compare Orleans to Concordia, a similar looking game that has a similar amount of systems and complexity. Similar to Orleans, Concordia has its own twist on deckbuilding + action selection. It also has coins and goods and a map, and purchasable special cards, and somewhat complex scoring. In Concordia however, all the systems tie together - each system's outputs feeds into the other systems. Coins and goods are a means to an end, and are in constant flux throughout the game. The variable map setup can meaningfully affect decisions throughout the game, and competition over desirable building spots is significant. Even though Concordia has a shorter rulebook, it makes Orleans look like a half-baked also-ran. 

Orleans is a nice game to play 3-5 times, don't get me wrong. The bag-building mechanic is nice. But I'd recommend getting those plays on a cafe copy, because I don't see this game having a long-term place in my collection. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6

RATING: 7.5
So far, [company=10848][/company] has been putting out a steady stream of family friendly strategy games. They all play well, have a nice mix of strategy and luck and fail to really stand out in the crowd. 
Orleans still isn't a heavy complex game, but it does have more depth than dlp's previous efforts so it's closer to my taste.
It combines bag building, worker placement and a number of other familiar mechanisms in a very organic way. Turns move fast and you're always doing stuff. There is very little downtime.
It also seems like there are multiple strategies to be explored.
If I have one nitpick it would be that while the game didn't take too long, it did feel like it had 1 or 2 rounds to many and it did start feeling slightly repetitive.
I quite liked it and I hope to play again soon.

RATING: 8
A point-salad, "bag-building" game that feels quite modern and streamlined. It's an enjoyable experience with lots to do on your turn, and yet the game moves along because the planning phase can be completed simultaneously with the other players -- a great innovation that cuts AP. With repeat plays, you'll learn the buildings and different strategies to focus on, which I'm hoping will increase enjoyment and justify my rating.

My worry is that the strategies will become self-evident and even obvious over time -- not necessarily a bad thing unless it's in a way that reduces charm and turns the game into a straightforward optimization exercise. Time will tell.

My first try was as a two-player experience. I'm eager to try it with more to find out if there are additional opportunities to block, or if it's really more of a multi-player solitaire game.

I also must say that I find the artwork, especially the medieval styled illustrations, charming!

RATING: 8
I probably shouldn't like Orleans because it's mostly a themeless euro with a side of point salad.  Yet somehow it really clicked with me.  The turns go by very quickly and there is a lot of simultaneous play, yet you still need to pay attention to what you opponents are doing.  I've always been a fan of bag building games, and it works here.  There seems to be a wide variety of strategies, especially because of the unique buildings you can earn.

I think my biggest complaint is that you get to search through the entire stack of buildings.  That means if there are some that are overpowered, you will go for those every time.  I kind of wish it was a "Draw 3, keep 1" situation where you still have a choice, but you're not guaranteed to get the building you want.

But that's a minor complaint.  This is a solid euro, and even though it plays kind of similarly to a Feld game (my least favorite designer), I enjoyed it.

RATING: 10
Orleans somehow manages to feel like a midweight to heavy euro but with the speed and ease of play of a much lighter game. It combines well euro mechanisms with deckbuilding. With the Trade and Intrigue expansion, culling (part of the fun and strategy of deckbuilding) plays an even more pivotal role.

I find it completely engrossing. It is tight, complex yet simple, and innovative. I'm constantly checking to see how many rounds are left, feeling as though I can't quite achieve everything I want to, but always managing to make something satisfying. And, checking also, because I don't want the experience to end.

Many 'deckbuilders' simply have cards that essentially do what they say, but Orleans gives you a multitude of options. And again, I think that's where it really shines, that it has the multitude of choice of a heavy euro mixed with the fun, fast paced mechanics of a deck builder (though a bag builder technically).

I love exploring different ways to build my engine (bag), trying different combinations of probabilities and how efficiently I can make each turn work for me. A marvellous game that will always have a place in my collection.

RATING: 7.5
A very well designed game that reminds me of a cross between Hyperborea (at a micro level) and Terra Mystica (at a macro level).

There are some aspects of the game that especially impressed me: 1) the graphic design is pretty much second to none (right up there with Eclipse), making the game extremely easy to learn and follow; 2) the game plays surprisingly quickly, thanks in large part to the simultaneous planning phase; 3) it seems very well-balanced in that you could try various strategies and not worry that you're not doing that ONE THING you need to focus on to win.

There is, however, one issue I have with the game that makes my preference lean slightly more towards Hyperborea: Orléans could desperately use an injection of player interaction.  The various tracks and the half-assed area control just aren't enough.  Orléans may very well be the better design, but Hyperborea is more fun to play.
