RATING: 7.5
Having played all three of the nominated games for the Kennerspiel des Jahres Award'15, I get the impression the jury has an agenda. Otherwise it's hard to believe Broom Service won over this one and Elysium.

This is a very fast-paced enjoyable game.Place workers, build up your deck/bag with more workers and then later retire these workers to improve your chances of drawing other workers which are getting more important during later parts of the game, collect goods, build trade posts... Nice part about thinning out your deck/bag is that you'll get rewarded for doing so. Like sending priests to the temple in Terra Mystica these "out of the game"-workers block spots other players' workers can't retire to and give you different advantages.Lots of viable strategies to pursue!
There's just a really minor criticism I could make. If you have a game that encourages a simultaneous action selection phase, why don't you include player screens (graphically fitting)? Maybe they're outdated, but I'd have appreciated a set.

RATING: 9
I own the deluxe version, my thoughts on the overall game:

It's excellent. The bag-building works really well and there are multiple paths you can take that all seem viable.

The game is very low on interaction/conflict. That's fine for me and most of the people I play with (and even preferred by some), but I know some people prefer more interaction.

RATING: 9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7

This is what is called a "Bag Builder" game. You start with four workers (who you can never lose) and through the game you acquire more workers which go into your bag. Every turn you draw workers, then place them on your player board. After everyone has placed, players take turns triggering actions. Actions can only be triggered for locations where all the appropriate workers have been placed. Actions that aren't completely filled won't trigger, but the workers can remain there until the action is triggered. After workers are triggered they return to your bag.

In addition to your personal player board, there's a communal map with many roads and waterways, all of which have a random assortment of goods on them. There's a set victory point value for the goods in the game, some are just better than others. Additionally, there are cities sprinkled about the board where players can build "guildhalls". The goods and guildhalls aren't used for anything during the game (mostly) and are only a way to generate points at the end of the game.

The actions you can trigger on your board are divided into a couple of different types. About half the actions give you a new worker and then something else. One action is related to purging workers, which gets you some reward. And the rest are related to the map. I don't remember the actual names of the locations, so I'll just describe what they do.

Get a Farmer - Gives you a new farmer and moves you up the farm track (which gives you better and better goods the more you progress)

Get a Sailor/Mechanic/Trader - Gives you a new work of the appropriate type and moves you up the matching track... the sailor track give you money, the mechanic gives you an automation tile which you can place to permanently obviate the need for a worker at a location and the Trader gives you a building which either gives you a power or is a new action spot that does something.

Get a Scholar - Gives you a scholar and moves you up the scholar track which gives you books. Books move you up on the development track which can be a way to get a lot of points. (At game end you'll score according to which development level you achieved by summing your citizen tokens and guildhalls and multiplying the total by your development level)

Get a Knight - Gives you a knight and moves you up the knight track which lets you draw more workers from your bag.

Get a Monk - Gets you a monk, which is a wild worker, but does nothing else.

Get a Book - Doesn't get you any worker, but gives you a book.

Purge - There's a third board in the game where you're building something? A church maybe? You can send up to two workers per turn to help build it. They are permanently committed to those spaces. The building project is divided into many different areas. Each gives a different reward (usually money, sometimes books) and there's a special reward (a citizen token) if you finish any particular area. The spots are keyed to certain worker types, so there might be a race to fill in certain spots before they disappear.

Travel by Wagon - Lets you move your meeple on the map and pick up one good that was on the path you took.

Travel by Ship - Lets you move your meeple on the map and pick up one good that was on the waterway you took.

Build a Guildhall - You can put your guildhall in the city you're in, assuming no one already has.

There are 18 random events (3 each of 6 types) and one will happen at the beginning of each turn. They mostly dock you for money, goods, or workers although some give you points or prevent you from hiring a monk.


The main challenge of the game is figuring out to effectively get and use your workers. Figuring out how to get points effectively is important. Automation is really useful as it makes it easier for you to trigger those actions. Getting buildings that help you or create points is important. You'll often have to make do with the workers you drew, so figuring out how to use what you've drawn can be a challenge. You're limited to having 8 workers in working memory, which doesn't count the ones assigned to locations in previous rounds, so you've got to juggle how many workers you'll use this turn, how many you'll draw next turn, how many you can keep unassigned and how many you need to commit to a location. So you might want to stay flexible and keep a few workers unassigned so that they can see what you draw next turn before committing them to some action... but if can draw a lot of workers then keeping some unassigned means you can't draw as many. But if you assign them to an action and then can't finish that action next turn (if you don't draw the right type of workers for example), you've tied yourself up a bit. And as you might be racing to pick up some good first, or take a particular spot while building the church, a delay might screw you. There are also citizen tokens tied to certain tracks, so not being able to claim an advance on a track can mean you lose out on that race as well.

There's a lot to think about in this game. It's often a challenge to balance taking the actions you want and how that changes the distribution of workers in your bag. Balancing how many workers you have in your bag and how many workers you pull in a round can be tricky too. Taking one action a lot will often mean you end up with a bag that's mostly one type of worker (which is generally bad). Taking a lot of workers into your makes it harder to pull all your workers and can make drawing the worker you need harder. You'll need to plan carefully to fare well in this game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
About [thing=164928][/thing]:

[b]1) What is it?[/b]
[thing=164928][/thing] is a pool building game, in the vein of [thing=36218][/thing] and [thing=91536][/thing], blended with worker placement. In it, you hire workers, that are represented by tokens, and the tokens go into your bag. In the next round, you will take a certain number of workers out of the bag and these will be available for use in that round - and you will use them mostly in your personal board, but they, by the use of the City Hall space on the personal board, can be sent to work in big issues involving the city of Orléans (they stay there - is basically the "trash" option).

With the workers (knights, craftsmen, farmers, monks, scholars, etc) you can help the city of Orléans, travel the country by land or river, build trade posts, acquire tools, adavance in science, and several other things.

[thing=164928][/thing] offers a nice experience, with many paths to follow, and all with a not big learning curve.

[b]2) How do you play?[/b]
Every round starts with the players pulling workers from the bag - at the start the number drawn is 4, but this can be increased by hiring Knights. Players allocate their workers, simultaneously, in spots in their own personal boards. There they might activate actions (hiring other workers, travel or building). The activation of actions is done one at a time, in clockwise order. This is important, as timming to do actions can be very important, since several spots on the country only have one good, which is taken by the first travelling there; citizens, that help the end game score, are taken by the first player to reach a certain spot or fill a task required by the city.

Workers can be left in the board from one round to the other, but there is a maximum space for 8  workers in the market (your available workers). Workers can also be left in a spot on the board, awaiting there to complete the action, but they can't be moved elsewhere.

The goal is to have the most points in the end - coins are points, goods are points and also there is a multiplication that is based in the number trading posts, citizens and level in the science track.

[b]3) Which are the decisions made during play?[/b]
There are several, but most not all that hard to be made. Which worker to hire (in two levels: for the immediate gain and, later, for what it can do); which building to take; where to add a tool (tools are tokens that cover spaces on the personal board, meaning that the worker under to tool is no longer needed in order to do the action); which path to travel; when to use the City Hall (not only to get rid of a unwanted worker, but also to gain coins or science, and the chance of getting a ciziten).

[thing=164928][/thing] mix long term strategy with tatical decisions all the time. You hire workers thinking in the now and/or for the future, then, at the start of the round, you draw randomly from the bag and must do the best you can with what you got. Tools and buildings are all in the strategy camp, and you won't be able to use them in the moment you acquire them, but they can make lots of difference later.

There is also a strong timming issue in the decision-making process. Some actions are better when you have the turn order on your side, doing something before someone else. And you can even set up this - you know that, next round, you will act before X player, therefore, you can delay something, or start something (like helping the city of Orléans) to finish it in the next round.

[b]4) What are the good things in the game?[/b]
- Smooth gameplay;
- Small downtime;
- Constant sense of advancement;
- Due to the way scoring works, with goods being hidden, and no one really counting coins, the decision of the game can be a tense moment, in order to discover the winner;
- Strategy mixed with tatic;
- Very good graphic design and art;
- Lots of decisions to be made throughout the game;
- Many paths to victory.

[b]5) Which are the bad news?[/b]
- Luck has its part in the game, mostly in the draw of workers from the bag, but also from the events, but there is ways to decrease the the random chance of the former and knowledge helps deal with the latter;
- The game should have player screens, like the ones in [thing=97207][/thing], as players allocate, at the same time, the workers (you can draw then in the open, just the selection should be done in secret). Doing in player order would just make for some huge and unnecessary downtime, and if doing at the same time, you or someone else can stall, in order to see what everyone is doing, or change, after you see someone, that will act first in the round, doing an action first, which will make yours moot or, at least, weaker. Player screens will solve all of this, so I recommend even DIY some.

[b]6) How do you feel while playing?[/b]
Like a successful person. Regardless of the path you choose to follow in the game, you will have some degree of success.

[thing=164928][/thing] provides a great sense of advancement, much greater than other deck/pool builders, since you don't just acquire a worker when you do this (like purchasing a card or a die), but you also gain a something immediately: hire a fisherman and you gain money; hire a knight and your "hand" of workers increase; hire a noble and you gain an unique building to use; hire a craftsman  and you gain a tool; and so on. Even when you travel, you gain a good along the way (if there is still any in the path). This works very well in passing a sense of accomplishment even if you are doing poorly, as you keep gaining things. 

Although punishing games can be really fun, it can be eerily like life, and can be nice to have unbirthday gifts coming almost every round.

[thing=164928][/thing] has a very nice design, offering multiple choices and allowing the players to pick their own path. With the simultaneous allocation of workers and the single action per turn, the downtime is minimal (there can still be some, though, if you are only doing one action, and the others are doing 3, 4, howeber, this doesn't happen often). There is a learning curve in the game, specially considering the timming of hiring, travelling and in knowing the events, but even someone playing for the first time, if the person has some prior experience with deck/pool building and work placement, will have a good chance of playing well. And once one learns it, it won't be required to, later, learn new cards, dice, effects and whatnot, which is usual for other deck/pool building games.

The time of playing is properly laid out: around 90 minutes if playing with 4, somewhat less with 3.

The graphic design works well, being easy to know what things do with a glance, and the game is language independant.

Overall, [thing=164928][/thing] goes well recommended.

RATING: 7
Let me come out and say that Orleans is a fun game. Folks that played the game all had fun. The game is quick, actions are relatively rapid and the game does not drag: In fact, the 18 rounds for each game while sounds intimidating, is actually a non-issue. That said, I remain slightly disappointed at the game after several plays. 

At the heart of my disappointment is the lack of alternate paths to victory. I am convinced that progressing on the development track and building trading posts just cannot be ignored completely in pursuit of other scoring options. Scoring from other sources just can't reach the same level of points as the trading post/dev track combo. Collecting goods alone is not sufficient to move ahead on the score.  Further, the event tiles also negatively hammer folks that focus on collecting goods by designating 3 types of tiles that impact goods collection whilst helping out folks that build the trading post and advancing on the development tracks. This can be remedied to a certain degree with the proper place tiles to expand the bonuses from goods collection. But there just aren't that many place tiles in the core set that encourages specialization for goods collection (those generating extra goods don't count).  Ultimately, this means the player that best balances all their scoring options will win the game.... certainly not the specialization to victory I was hoping to see.

All that adds up to a game that is good but could really be better. I can't help but feel that with a few tweaks, other paths can developed to compete with trading post/dev track option.

RATING: 10

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Orleans is a riff on deck-building, but instead of using decks of cards, it uses a chit pull system folks are calling bag-building. The game is incredibly easy to both teach and understand. You simply pull a set number of chits and match them to a space which usually allows you to collect another chit to add to your bag. Eventually certain combinations of chits begin feeding actions that gather victory points.

Is this easy? Yes. At first it seemed almost too easy to be interesting. What I did not anticipate, was the subtle difference between drawing chits from a bag and running through a deck of cards. In a traditional deck-builder, you know that you eventually see all the cards in your deck and you can plan accordingly. In bag-building world of Orleans though, after every turn your used pieces go back into the bag. What this means is that it is entirely possible to see the same pieces every single turn or alternatively, to never draw the same piece twice. Either of which can be maddening as you try to plan and execute big turns, or even timely ones for that matter.

In other words, Orleans is simple but it's not easy. That fine distinction makes it a very nice addition to any collection. Games you can teach in five minutes that stay interesting after a couple of plays are a pretty rare commodity after all. There are a few predetermined paths through the game, but if you find players relying on them, the game does provide a nice variant for changing the available buildings, which works even better if you pick up the promos from the geek store.

RATING: 5
We accidentally played the game wrong, and it seems it was better for it. You're supposed to do your actions one action at a time in player order. we just did them all at once, mostly simultaneously. It seems to me the game got at least an hour shorter because of that, and it would be too long for what it is and how much luck is in it the official way.
Anyway, the game feels good to play, but there are some things that don't really click for me. Compared to Hyperborea another bagbuilding game, here you don't really have to count what's left in your bag, but on the other hand there is no guarantee to draw everything that's in your bag, because you will immediately put back used tokens in the bag. This creates and additional luck factor too.
I don't really feel like the events add anything to the game, other than more luck factor via the Plague.
The travelling, building houses and collecting goods part is also meaningless. It feels the designer came up with the bagbuilding part, and struggled to add a game to it.
The town hall is weird. I really like the idea of getting rid of your tokens for points, but getting a huge bonus for finishing a set is nothing more than oppurtunity for accidental kingmaking. I didn't really watch out for that, accidentally giving someone 12 points. I guess it's supposed to add interaction to a pretty solitaire game but ... come on. There are better ways for that.

RATING: 7
Enjoyable euro game with a unique bag-building mechanics. The rules are rather simple, but playing this game effectively and efficiently is by no means easy. It will take a while to fully unlock the potential of Orleans and learn the intricacies of the interplay between various tiles and actions. I do have some concerns regarding the long-term replayability of the game due to the static setup of the building tiles, but hopefully the expansions will help with that. So far so good.

The downside I've found is that certain moves and strategies seem like no-brainers while others (including some building tiles) are rather useless (or at least I don't think I would ever buy them). Also, the Town Hall board doesn't scale at all so that it's very difficult grab Citizens that way in a 2-player game. But although some parts of this design feel rather unpolished, I still enjoy the game, it's a lot of fun. I wish it had more player interaction though.

/update: I wish I hadn't sold the base game as Trade & Intrigue expansion seems to fix every issue I had with it. I will have to check it out someday.

RATING: 6
going to have a hard time not comparing this to Hyperborea. Both games feature a bag-building mechanic where you’re acquiring cubes or discs and putting them into a bag, “shuffling” the bag, and then drawing a certain number of cubes or discs. Using those cubes or discs, you then take actions depending on the color. In both games, you can leave those cubes or discs on the board between rounds in order to leave them out of the bag and as a way of making it easier to take an action later. The biggest difference is that you have to purposefully reset in Hyperborea and you just take the discs from used actions in Orléans.

So, what’s my take on Orléans? Well, I think it’s the better game. Here’s why it’s better:

More fluid, faster game
Better art, though I prefer Hyperborea’s theme.
Fantastic components
Faster setup (though, not by a ton)
Really though, after playing a very long “short game” of Hyperborea last night, the play length is probably the thing that most appeals to me. Now, I do enjoy long games, but my patience for overly long games is running very thin lately. I don’t get to play games as much as I’d like and wasting time on a game that feels like it should be over faster is a cardinal sin in my book. Orléans really does feel like it moves quicker and with more interesting decisions and less analysis paralysis.

This makes it sound like play length is everything, but it’s not. Orléans is a fantastic game in it’s own right. It takes what made deck building games interesting and removed the cards without adding too many more mechanics. The theme—medieval life—is overdone, but I’m okay with it considering how well it’s drawn thanks to Klemens Franz.

Out of all of the new euro games I’ve played recently, this has been one of the best.

Highly recommended.

RATING: N/A
Medieval euro, "pouch builder" worker placement point salad with lots of resource conversion. Standard and special workers are randomly drawn each round from a bag of tokens. In deck builder style players can hire additional workers to add to the bag. Many scoring paths; census track for attracting farmers, progress tracks for the special workers, travel around the region and establish guild houses and trade routes, produce resources, build buildings, and sacrifice worker tokens to community development projects.  It is a sprawling game that needs a lot of table space. So many things I like about this game; limited resources, simultaneous programmed actions, automation concept from using craftsman, the wildcard of the monk, the deck thinning element offered by sending workers to community improvements, dual use of countdown timer tiles that cleverly symbolize passage of and remaining game rounds while causing a bane or boon event. The color coding of the board and pieces makes it visually easy and clear to teach and to plan your actions. One thing I like most is this is one of the few point salad games that offers many paths to score points that can actually lead to victory. Our experience is most games of this category have several scoring options but only one or two ways to win. Aside from limited resources and turn order in placing guilds and trade routes it is not very interactive and could be called multi player solitaire but I really enjoy this game and see this moving up the BGG ranks fast.

RATING: 8
Initial rating, but it may go up. I really liked the deluxe components and the ease of play. Fairly simple to understand the benefit of various placement spots, but there is a great deal of variety in terms of paths to pursue for endgame points.

As some have said, there is randomness in what comes out and what -- possibly -- gets removed from your bag. I was hit by this in the first game somewhat, but I still think it's a necessary element to keep things interesting.

Certain strategies seem apparent after the first play, but I'd certainly enjoy trying again. Fun to optimize your workers or setup bigger plays on subsequent rounds.

Definitely prone to large amounts of AP, especially at five players. That was my main issue, especially when dealing with a stalled engine and others making four or five plays in one turn.

RATING: 8
Maybe I shouldn't rate it a 9 after one play, but it truly is a great game. The bag building mechanic is interesting. I'm a big fan of the map that you're roaming around on.

A few strange negatives: the techs feel a little weird, and I think removing half of them each game is good. Also, during the planning phase, why does everyone go at the same time? Strange. During the early game, this doesn't matter, but it sure does later on. And the suggestion in the rules to do this in turn order is absurd. The game needs shields so you can plan in secret.

I see that I've written plenty more about the negatives than the positives, but the game is truly great, and I can't wait to play it again.

RATING: 7
Orleans is a really nice mix of comfortable mechanics.  Most notably the "bag building" of your worker chips has a feel similar to deck building without being the classic Dominion formula.  In fact you are trying to seed your bad with the right pieces for the set collectionish worker management phase which is the real heart of the game.  This part combined with your ability to improve your action choices is the heart of the game and gives you a big action space to play in with lots of options to score points. Of course the game is also pretty sprawling with lots of inherent fiddliness.  

So all in all an enjoyable game... but for now I don't feel a need to add it to my collection.  Just a bit too much work to set up and explain for what is in the end just another engine building Euro.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is a game with legs. Every time you play, the board changes enough to create new challenges and strategies. Many of the buildings are quite powerful and a force to be reckoned with. The much maligned Bathhouse is no more powerful than the School, Herb Garden, or the Laboratory as powerful cornerstones of various strategies (and that's based on a handful of plays). The end of turn events are never fully destructive but being unprepared will catch players out. Choosing to take a new follower for its bonus action still saddles you with another follower that may be drawn at the beginning of your turn, which can clog up your works. Definitely spring for the wooden upgrades (meeples or cylindrical discs), as it makes drawing pieces much easier (the small discs get lost in the corners of the bag). All in all, this game obviously has legs and will take many, many plays to even begin to master.
